AI at the Edge: S3 E2

AMD(677.0K) ·30:04 ·732 views · 2026-04-15
Mark Papermaster (CTO, AMD) Ramin Hasani (Co-founder and CEO, Liquid AI)
Liquid AI is pioneering a new class of "Liquid Foundation Models" (LFMs) that are two orders of magnitude smaller than traditional cloud-based models, enabling high-performance, proactive AI agents to run entirely offline on edge devices like AI PCs.
TL;DR Liquid AI is pioneering a new class of "Liquid Foundation Models" (LFMs) that are two orders of magnitude smaller than traditional cloud-based models, enabling high-performance, proactive AI agents to run entirely offline on edge devices like AI PCs. Brain-Inspired...

TL;DR

Liquid AI is pioneering a new class of "Liquid Foundation Models" (LFMs) that are two orders of magnitude smaller than traditional cloud-based models, enabling high-performance, proactive AI agents to run entirely offline on edge devices like AI PCs.

Architectural Innovation: Liquid Neural Networks (LNNs)

  • Brain-Inspired Computation: Liquid AI moved away from standard transformer architectures to create LNNs based on how neurons exchange information in nature.
      • Efficiency Gains: The company aims to shrink models from the trillion-parameter regime used in the cloud to a 100M to 10B parameter range without sacrificing quality.
      • Alternative Primitives: Unlike transformers that rely almost exclusively on matrix multiplication, LFMs explore diverse mathematical operators to optimize for memory and speed.
  • Hardware-in-the-Loop Design: The team uses a meta-AI system to search for the most efficient computational graph for a specific processor (CPU, NPU, or GPU).
      • Performance Metrics: This approach targets a memory footprint as small as 1GB while achieving latencies as low as 20 milliseconds for large language model (LLM) tasks.

The Shift to Proactive Edge AI

  • Reactive vs. Proactive Agents: Current AI often waits for a prompt (reactive); Liquid AI enables "proactive agents" that run continuously in the background to perform tasks on a user’s behalf.
  • On-Device Advantages:
      • Privacy and Security: By running models locally on Ryzen AI PCs, users can process sensitive data (financials, health records, or secret projects) in "air-gapped" environments.
      • Latency and Reliability: Local execution eliminates the need for an internet connection and avoids the latency spikes of cloud-based APIs.
      • Multimodality: LFMs are designed to handle vision, text, and audio—noting that low latency is particularly critical for natural audio interactions.

Power Efficiency and Sustainability

  • NPU Optimization: Liquid AI works closely with AMD to optimize workloads for Neural Processing Units (NPUs), which provide high-compute throughput (50-60 TOPs) at significantly lower power than traditional chips.
  • Sustainability: By "juicing out" maximum intelligence per watt at the algorithmic level, Liquid AI addresses the global energy crisis created by the massive power demands of traditional generative AI data centers.
  • Societal Impact: Mark Papermaster highlights that driving down the cost and power requirements of AI is a form of "democratization," making advanced intelligence accessible to regions or industries without massive infrastructure.

Ecosystem and the Future of Co-Design

  • Open Source Commitment: Liquid AI releases its models as "open weights," reporting over 1 million downloads per week as of early 2024.
  • Market Scale: Ramin Hasani estimates that out of the 35 billion processors manufactured annually, approximately 2 billion (across phones, laptops, and cars) are currently capable of hosting LFMs.
  • Hardware-Software Co-Design: The ultimate vision is a "holistic design" where the foundation model layer is so deeply integrated into the silicon that the chip itself is inherently "agent-aware."
"We are talking about shrinking down the size of intelligence to the point, without sacrificing quality, that you can deliver the same quality of those cloud models on the device... that required a change of model architecture and thinking about other primitives of computations." — Ramin Hasani
"If an AI system can run in the background... you can enable something I call proactive agents. They can perform a job that you actually give to the system and they can really be an agent on your behalf—an army of assistants." — Ramin Hasani
"Taking the type of capabilities and agentic processing that today can only be run in the cloud, right into PCs and other very, very efficient devices... it's basically a democratization, making AI available to the masses." — Mark Papermaster

On this episode, I'm joined by Ramin Hasani, co-founder and CEO of Liquid AI, and a machine learning scientist at the Computer Science and Artificial Intelligence Lab, or CSAIL, at MIT. And I particularly love it because you're rethinking how to optimize, like how AMD as a chip developer can work with you as a practitioner of the state-of-the-art AI to create this whole new class of AI computation. It is great to have you in the show, and I would love to hear just your thoughts about Liquid AI, your startup, and how things are going. Thanks for having me; likewise, very much looking forward to this conversation. Yes, so Liquid AI, we are a foundation model company. We are building efficient foundation models. As you mentioned, we are rethinking how we design foundation models. It is not just about quality. We're thinking about where intelligence goes, how AI is getting served, where AI is getting served, what latency you are getting out of the AI feature that you're hosting, and how much battery are you consuming. You know, there is a whole world of AI and processors that exist outside of data centers. So we're talking about the device world, right? And this is where Liquid AI wants to focus on and really double down on that kind of market, which is kind of untapped. Not a lot of attention goes into the device world because there are a lot of technological challenges and breakthroughs that had to happen for us to be able to truly enable the same level of generative AI kind of powers that you see on the cloud to actually enable those things also on the device world. That was where we came in essentially three years ago, spun out of the MIT Computer Science and Artificial Intelligence Lab, CSAIL. We started this company building on my PhD thesis and my co-founders' decade-long work that we have been working on a concept called Liquid Neural Networks. The idea there was that let's take a step back and let's look into what nature has taught us in terms of computational units that are used in, let's say, the brain of species and in animals. In small animals, we take a physical approach to bring a little bit more insights from how neurons perform computation in brains, and take those inspirations to the machine learning world. The whole thesis around this was the fact that we want to pack a lot more intelligence into smaller units of computation. For example, we wanted to power robots. The processors there are not the luxury of a very beefy GPU. But there you have a processor that is smaller and you need to be really counting for every intelligence per watt that you are actually optimizing for, right? And so that was kind of the domain, and at the same time, this is a physical world. We are talking about bringing intelligence into the physical world and onto smaller and low-power processors. So then we invented this technology, Liquid Neural Networks, on the principles of how two neurons exchange information with each other. That was the start of the process. Modeling that allowed us to build very advanced AI systems for predictive robotics applications, predictive finance, and predictive healthcare. We showed at MIT during our research how much you can actually do. We showed that with a handful of neurons, you can drive a car completely on a very, very little processor. If at the unit of computation these models are extremely smaller than the alternative artificial neural networks that are solving the similar problem, maybe at scale we can keep these scaling laws the same way and power generative AI and power new forms of computation. Ramin, that is so exciting. And I'd, I'd like to dive a little deeper into exactly that. And just for our listeners, I'd like to highlight the huge contrast of the innovation you just described for LFMs versus what they're all running on today when they go in the cloud. So if they go in the cloud as you said, beefy GPUs, these are trillion-parameter foundational models with this vast knowledge base that's been trained. I think we could dive a little bit deeper and folks would be very interested in that. You know, whenever a technology actually starts, you always have the exploration phase, then we start learning from that kind of space and we try to make that process a little bit more efficient. So that efficiency—when we understand and mature in a certain technological trend—you can see how that affects efficiency. There is definitely kind of a trend towards making models more efficient and making models move towards being smaller but more powerful without loss of quality. So intelligence per watt as a kind of a measure is going up, you know, so this is what is definitely happening. But what we are talking about at Liquid AI is two orders of magnitude even smaller than what you just mentioned. We are talking about going from the trillion-parameter regime to the 100 million or basic between 1 billion to 10 billion kind of parameter regime. We are talking about really shrinking down the size of intelligence to the point, without sacrificing quality, that you can deliver the same quality of those cloud models directly on a device. And that required two different shifts of thoughts and algorithms. Like we have done innovations across model architecture—that change of model architecture and thinking about other primitives of computations. For example, perhaps 99% of the generative AI today is powered by the transformer architecture, right? Or variations of this transformer architecture. But that's just matrix multiplication and ways that we can actually figure out how to perform matrix multiplication. There are so many other mathematical operators that you can actually start exploring and seeing: can we build general-purpose behavior with other types of, or maybe a combination of other mathematical operators? That was an objective function at Liquid AI. We wanted to see can we get, let's say, tiny models that optimize for memory. Let's say they can fit in 1 gigabyte of memory, which is a very, very tiny space if you think about it in terms of today's models and generative AI models. We reduce the memory size, increase the speed, and reduce the latency of the model while keeping the quality of the model at its highest kind of capacity on the job that they want to perform. But then thinking it from first principles, two innovations had to happen as I was mentioning. One of them was this model architecture that I alluded to. The other thing was that we thought about a systematic approach on changing the architecture. We thought about building a meta AI system that allows us to build a search space of possibilities, put the processor of use in the loop of that search space, and then go through basically seeing what should be the computational graph of a foundation model for a given processor. Imagine if we put an AMD CPU or an NPU or a GPU, like a consumer GPU level. You're talking about Ryzen AI PCs for example, right? We are putting one of those units in the loop. Then what we try to do, we try to have a search algorithm to look over variations of computational graphs that run really fast. From the speed point of view, they are extremely optimized. From the latency point of view, they are optimized, and at the same time from the memory footprint point of view, they are extremely optimized while they are satisfying certain benchmarks. For example, if you're talking about a knowledge worker that is powered on a laptop, you would be able to have a Liquid Foundation Model designed for those CPU, GPU, and NPU classes. And that architecture is going to be the most efficient variant of a foundation model that you can ever get that is performing that job as good as the cloud models. So enabling this hardware-in-the-loop approach that we took in designing foundation models and thinking fundamentally about different operators that can enable general-purpose computer—these were the two innovations upon which we built Liquid Foundation Models. And this is now enabling a completely new world. This new world of device foundation models. You can bring now all sorts of technologies, all sorts of exciting opportunities for AI agents that you've seen in the cloud, directly onto the device. You know, use them for offline applications, for privacy-critical applications, for decision-critical applications, and for security air-gapped kind of environments. These are kind of the range of opportunities that is fantastic, you know, and I'm super excited about it. I am too. In fact, I actually wanna dive a little deeper into that. You spoke at the Consumer Electronics Show along with Lisa Su as she hosted the opening keynote. If you could just share some of the use cases that are there now and what you see coming even in the next few months. You've seen the whole world of AI since the beginning of 2026 got dominated by AI agents and then sub-agents. You saw things like OpenDevin coming out in January. But even before that, with AMD and with Lisa and your team, we've been really working on this idea of not just bringing reactive AI agents onto processors. Because if you bring an AI system that runs directly on a laptop, there is no reason for these systems not to be active all the time. So if an AI system can run in the background of the system—on and in the background of a computer—you can think about all sorts of productivity tasks. Currently, you are reactively using AI like you're going to a chatbot, you're trying to complete a job, and then the AI reacts and comes back to you. But now AIs can become a lot more proactive. If AIs can consistently run on the device without interruption and they are low power—so they are not damaging the battery and the energy is controlled—then now you can enable something that I call proactive agents. They can perform a job that you actually give to the system and they can really be an agent on your behalf. Think about an assistant; think about an army of assistants, you know? So we're talking about agents and sub-agents being able to perform a lot of jobs for you. And that's the era that we are living in. That's why success for these use cases has been where the backend is one of the big AIs, like ones from Gemini, from OpenAI, or from Anthropic. Imagine if you could enable that on the device, directly on the device. That is the world that we're enabling. So we're talking about building brains that can sit on the laptop directly. So whenever you're even offline, whenever you want to give it some sensitive information about your health, about your taxes, about your financial situation, or about your secret project that you're working on that you don't want to share with the cloud, you can have all of those things basically run directly on the device. And then enabling that agentic behavior, the same type of behavior that you're seeing from sub-agents and agents like taking a job and doing it for you—doing that directly on the device. That's the world that we are enabling basically with the Liquid Foundation Models. To take those types of agentic flows and to bring them into your device, to bring them into like an AI PC and run it so productively, I think it's gonna be a game-changer. And we're just at the beginning. It can run offline and you can trust it. The reason why you have to run it on NPU for sustained workload is that NPUs are kind of the low-power place where you can compute with lowest possible power. We are talking about having almost a supercomputer on your hand. What we are trying to do is bring Liquid Foundation Models directly running and sustaining a job on your NPUs. And we are not just talking about one Liquid Foundation Model. We are talking about an army of Liquid Foundation Models. Each of them is specialized for doing something on top of your computer. We're talking about designing for processors. Our approach allows us to have a hardware-in-the-loop and processor-in-the-loop design for these foundation models. For example, if you think about even data center GPUs, our models run extremely fast even on GPUs that are hosted in the data centers. White House Press Briefing

C-SPAN(1.8M) ·40:45 ·8.5K views · 2026-04-15
Karoline Leavitt (White House Press Secretary) Scott Bessent (Treasury Secretary) Kelly Loeffler (Administrator, Small Business Administration)
On Tax Day, the administration highlighted the impact of the "Working Families Tax Cuts," reporting an average refund of $3,400 and significant participation in new tax exemptions for tips and overtime.
TL;DR On Tax Day, the administration highlighted the impact of the "Working Families Tax Cuts," reporting an average refund of $3,400 and significant participation in new tax exemptions for tips and overtime. Strategically, the White House is maintaining a naval blockade and...

TL;DR

On Tax Day, the administration highlighted the impact of the "Working Families Tax Cuts," reporting an average refund of $3,400 and significant participation in new tax exemptions for tips and overtime. Strategically, the White House is maintaining a naval blockade and "maximum pressure" sanctions on Iran, characterizing short-term energy price volatility as a necessary trade-off for global security.

Individual Tax Relief and IRS Performance

  • Tax Cut Participation: Approximately 53 million filers (45% of all returns) claimed at least one of the administration’s signature tax cuts.
      • No Tax on Overtime: Claimed by over 25 million filers, with an average deduction of $3,100.
      • No Tax on Tips: Utilized by over 6 million service workers, averaging a $7,100 deduction.
      • Senior Benefits: Over 30 million seniors utilized enhanced deductions, averaging $7,000.
  • Refund Statistics: The average refund for the current filing season is over $3,400. The administration claims these are the largest refunds in U.S. history.
  • Withholding Strategy: Treasury Secretary Scott Bessent encouraged Americans to adjust their withholdings to receive an "automatic real wage increase" on a monthly basis rather than waiting for an annual refund.
  • Trump Accounts: Five million families have opened these accounts for their children, with 1.2 million eligible for a $1,000 bonus. The program aims to increase national financial literacy and long-term savings.

Small Business and Industrial Reinvestment

  • Economic Impact: SBA Administrator Kelly Loeffler reported that 12 million small businesses saved an average of $7,000 this year.
  • Key Policy Drivers:
      • Pass-Through Deduction: The 20% small business pass-through deduction is now permanent, which the SBA credits for the creation of one million new jobs.
      • 100% Expensing: Provisions for equipment and R&D expensing are being used to "re-industrialize" the U.S. manufacturing base.
  • Small Business Optimism: Despite a recent dip in March data, Loeffler asserted that the long-term trend remains above the 50-year average, contrasting it with 34 consecutive months of below-average optimism during the previous administration.

Energy and International Sanctions

  • Gas Price Outlook: Secretary Bessent projected that gas prices would return to the "$3.00 range" between June 20 and September 20. He noted that while crude oil prices have dropped recently, retail stations have been slow to lower prices.
  • Iran Blockade and Sanctions: The U.S. is enforcing a full naval blockade on all vessels entering or departing Iranian ports.
      • Strategic Sacrifice: Press Secretary Karoline Leavitt stated the administration is willing to accept "short-term volatility" in energy prices to prevent Iran from obtaining a nuclear weapon.
      • Secondary Sanctions: The Treasury has issued warnings to two Chinese banks, threatening secondary sanctions if they continue to process payments for Iranian oil.
  • Mediation: Pakistan remains the sole mediator in ongoing negotiations with Iran; the administration denied reports that they had requested an extension of the current cease-fire.

Justice Department and National Commemoration

  • Attorney General Transition: Todd Blanche is currently serving as the acting Attorney General. Leavitt emphasized that the President’s top priority for the permanent role is "accountability for the weaponization of justice."
  • U.S. Triumphal Arc: The administration announced plans to build a 250-foot tall "United States Triumphal Arc" in Washington, D.C. (Logan Circle) to commemorate the 250th anniversary of American independence. Construction is slated to begin this year.

***

Notable Quotes

"No tax on overtime. It is the American way. If you want to work longer hours, you can keep more money."

— Scott Bessent, Treasury Secretary

"Small business is big business. They are a massive economic engine that creates two-thirds of all new jobs... the working family tax cuts are putting money back into the pockets of the hardest working Americans."

— Kelly Loeffler, SBA Administrator

"The President has been clear: this is short-term disruption for the long-term strategic goal of the United States to ensure the world's leading state sponsor of terror cannot obtain a nuclear weapon."

— Karoline Leavitt, White House Press Secretary

On C-SPAN3 at Dream City Church, I'm honored to be standing here today on Tax Day with two esteemed members of the President's cabinet and his excellent economic team: Treasury Secretary Scott Bessent and Small Business Administrator Kelly Loeffler. As we have seen this tax season, President Trump's Working Families Tax Cuts have put a historic amount of money back in the pockets of the American people this year. On the campaign trail in 2024, President Trump looked voters in the eye and promised he would sign the largest tax cuts in the history of our country. Last Independence Day, President Trump and Congressional Republicans delivered, passing the largest tax cut in history for working and middle-class Americans, including no tax on tips, overtime, and Social Security. They passed unprecedented tax relief for small businesses, farmers, workers, and families. With Tax Day officially here, the numbers prove these tax cuts have been nothing short of extraordinary. More than 53 million filers claimed at least one of President Trump's signature new tax cuts. The average refund of the filing season is more than $3,400. The Trump administration has issued more refunds and larger refunds than at any year in the history of our country. I will allow Secretary Bessent to break down the numbers in greater detail. President Trump and Republicans in Congress have always believed the American people know how to spend their money better than the government does. What the American people must not forget is that every single Democrat in Congress voted against these tax refunds. The media has largely omitted this fact and other critical details in their stories covering the benefits of the Trump tax cuts. If the Democrats in Congress had gotten their way and blocked President Trump's historic legislation, Americans would have faced the largest tax hike in history: nearly $4 trillion. The average taxpayer would have seen an 822% increase in their tax rates—a 22% increase in their tax rates. They have proven they are affordability frauds who believe the government should keep more of the American people's hard-earned money. This President and Republicans in Congress fundamentally believe Americans deserve to keep more of their hard-earned paychecks. It is a clear contrast for American families to keep in mind. I will turn it over to our great Treasury Secretary, Scott Bessent, then he will turn it over to Kelly. Thank you. SEC. BESSENT: Thank you. I'm here to tell you that it has been a fantastic tax season. It has gone smoothly and gone efficiently from the point of view of the IRS. Most importantly, it's been a great tax season for the American people thanks to promises made and promises kept through the Working Families Tax Cuts. The President has delivered record refunds to the American people. 45% of the filers of the returns we have seen have had at least one of the four policies: no tax on tips, no tax on overtime, deductions for great seniors, and deductibility of interest on auto loans for American-made cars. I will tell you the biggest—one of those that Americans have taken is no tax on overtime. It is the American way. If you want to work longer hours, you can keep more money. What we are seeing—I want to encourage everyone out there watching today to change their withholding if they have not already done so. If you change your withholding, you will get an automatic real wage increase on a weekly or monthly basis. You will be able to keep more of your money this calendar year. We have also seen with the Trump accounts, we have had 5 million families file for Trump accounts for their children. 1.2 million are eligible for the $1,000 bonus. We look forward to that picking up more and more. For those who have not done it, it's easy to remember: Form 4547. You can do it while you're filing your taxes or at any point during the year. We think this will be transformational in terms of financial literacy. Financial literacy at Treasury and all over the administration—we are working with the American people, with American families, not only to give them more money but to show them how to invest it in a safe, smart, and sound way. I will turn it over to Kelly. MS. LOEFFLER: Thank you, Scott. Good afternoon everyone. Excited to be here on Tax Day. It is an important day for small business because small business and their employees have real relief thanks to President Trump's Working Family Tax Cuts. As Administrator of the Small Business Administration, it's my honor to serve our great President as a cabinet-level voice for America's 36 million small businesses. President Trump is right: small business is big business. They are a massive economic engine that creates two-thirds of all new jobs. Jensen Huang – Will Nvidia’s moat persist?

Dwarkesh Patel(1.3M) ·103:13 ·208.9K views · 2026-04-15
Jensen Huang (CEO, NVIDIA) Dwarkesh Patel (Host, Dwarkesh Patel Podcast)
Nvidia CEO Jensen Huang argues that Nvidia’s moat is built on "accelerated computing" rather than mere chip manufacturing, leveraging a 20-year head start in the CUDA ecosystem to deliver 50x performance gains through algorithmic co-design.
TL;DR Nvidia CEO Jensen Huang argues that Nvidia’s moat is built on "accelerated computing" rather than mere chip manufacturing, leveraging a 20-year head start in the CUDA ecosystem to deliver 50x performance gains through algorithmic co-design. He warns that aggressive export...

TL;DR

Nvidia CEO Jensen Huang argues that Nvidia’s moat is built on "accelerated computing" rather than mere chip manufacturing, leveraging a 20-year head start in the CUDA ecosystem to deliver 50x performance gains through algorithmic co-design. He warns that aggressive export controls on China risk ceding the world's second-largest market and forcing a shift toward non-American technology standards, potentially undermining long-term U.S. leadership.

The Business of "Electrons to Tokens"

  • Fundamental Model: Huang defines Nvidia’s core mission as the transformation of "electrons to tokens," where the "token" (a unit of intelligence) is the new valuable molecule of the digital economy.
  • "As Much as Needed, As Little as Possible": Nvidia’s philosophy is to focus only on the "insanely hard" parts of the stack while partnering for everything else.
      • Hyperscaler Strategy: Nvidia avoids becoming a cloud provider/hyperscaler because the market is already well-served; instead, they focus on backstopping "neoclouds" (e.g., CoreWeave, Crusoe, Nebius) to ensure their architecture remains the industry standard.
  • The Software Explosion: Contrary to fears that AI will commoditize software, Huang predicts the number of software instances will skyrocket as AI agents—not just humans—become the primary users of tools like Excel and Synopsys.

Supply Chain as a Strategic Moat

  • Capital Commitments: Reports indicate Nvidia has between $100 billion and $250 billion in purchase commitments with foundries (TSMC) and memory suppliers (SK Hynix, Micron).
  • Informing Upstream Investment: Huang’s "moat" involves persuading CEOs of supply chain partners to invest in capacity (like CoWoS packaging) years in advance by demonstrating massive downstream demand.
  • Bottleneck Resolution:
      • Scaling Timeframes: Most hardware bottlenecks (logic, memory, CoWoS) are 2-3 year problems that resolve once a clear demand signal is provided.
      • The Energy Constraint: Huang identifies energy policy—not chip fabrication—as the primary long-term bottleneck for "reindustrializing" the U.S. and building AI factories.
      • Human Capital: He expresses concern over "doomers" discouraging careers in software engineering or radiology, noting that AI increases the demand for experts rather than replacing them.

Competitive Edge: CUDA vs. ASICs (TPUs)

  • Programmability vs. Fixed Logic: While TPUs and ASICs (Application-Specific Integrated Circuits) are optimized for matrix multiplication, Huang argues Nvidia's "accelerated computing" is superior because it is programmable.
      • Algorithmic Leaps: Nvidia achieved a 50x jump from Hopper to Blackwell—far exceeding Moore’s Law—by simultaneously optimizing processors, fabric (NVLink), and libraries.
  • TCO and Market Reach: Huang claims Nvidia offers the best Total Cost of Ownership (TCO) and "performance-per-watt," challenging competitors like Amazon (Trainium) and Google (TPU) to prove their cost-advantage claims via public benchmarks like MLPerf.
  • The Investment "Miss": Huang admits a previous mistake in not realizing that early AI labs (OpenAI, Anthropic) required massive investment from suppliers. Nvidia is now correcting this by participating in multi-billion dollar funding rounds for major labs.

Geopolitical Strategy and the China Debate

  • The Danger of Conceding Markets: Huang strongly opposes total export bans on China, arguing that conceding 40% of the world's technology market forces China to build its own ecosystem (e.g., Huawei’s record growth) and accelerates domestic chip innovation.
  • Energy as an Offset: While the U.S. has superior 3nm/2nm chips, Huang notes that China’s abundance of energy and manufacturing capacity allows them to compensate for using older 7nm technology by simply "ganging up" more chips.
  • The "American Tech Stack": He argues it is in the U.S. national interest for the world’s researchers (50% of whom are Chinese) to develop their models on American hardware and standards.
      • Standardization Risk: If Chinese-developed open-source models are optimized for domestic hardware, the "American tech stack" loses its global dominance as AI diffuses to the Global South.

Notable Quotes

"Our job is to do as much as necessary and as little as possible to enable that transformation [electrons to tokens] to be done at incredible capabilities." — Jensen Huang
"Nvidia's GPUs, accelerators, are like F1 racers. It takes quite a bit of expertise to be able to push it to the limit... we use a ton of AI to create the kernels that we have." — Jensen Huang
"If we scare this country into thinking that AI is somehow a nuclear bomb... I don't know how you're helping the United States. You're doing it a disservice." — Jensen Huang
"Moore's Law is dead. Between Hopper and Blackwell, from the transistors themselves, call it 75% [improvement]... Blackwell is 50 times Hopper. My point is: architecture matters. Computer science matters." — Jensen Huang
We've seen the valuations of a bunch of software companies crash because people are expecting AI to commoditize software. There's a potentially naive way of thinking about things, which is: look, Nvidia sends a GDS2 file to TSMC. TSMC builds the logic dies, it builds the switches, then it packages them with the HBM that SK Hynix, Micron, and Samsung make. Then it sends it to an ODM in Taiwan where they assemble the racks. Nvidia is fundamentally making software that other people are manufacturing, and if software gets commoditized, does Nvidia get commoditized? In the end, something has to transform electrons to tokens. The transformation of electrons to tokens and making those tokens more valuable over time is hard to completely commoditize. The transformation from electrons to tokens is such an incredible journey. Making that token is like making one molecule more valuable than another molecule, making one token more valuable than another. The amount of artistry, engineering, science, and invention that goes into making that token valuable, obviously we're watching it happen in real time. The transformation, the manufacturing, all of the science that goes in there is far from deeply understood and the journey is far from over. I doubt that it will happen. We're going to make it more efficient, of course. The way that you framed the question is my mental model of our company. The input is electrons, the output is tokens. In the middle is Nvidia. Our job is to do as much as necessary and as little as possible to enable that transformation to be done at incredible capabilities. What I mean by "as little as possible," whatever I don't need to do, I partner with somebody and make it part of my ecosystem. If you look at Nvidia today, we probably have the largest ecosystem of partners, both in the supply chain upstream and downstream, all of the computer companies, application developers, and model makers. AI is a five-layer cake, if you will. We have ecosystems across the entire five layers. We try to do as little as possible, but the part that we have to do, as it turns out, is insanely hard. I don't think that gets commoditized. In fact, I also don't think the enterprise software companies, the tools makers… Most software companies today are tool makers. Some of them are not. Some of them are workflow codification systems. But for a lot of companies, they're tool makers. For example, Excel is a tool, PowerPoint is a tool, Cadence makes tools, Synopsys makes tools. I actually see the opposite of what people see. I think the number of agents is going to grow exponentially, and the number of tool users is going to grow exponentially. It's very likely that the number of instances of all these tools is going to skyrocket. It’s very likely that the number of instances of Synopsys Design Compiler is going to skyrocket, along with the number of agents using the floor planners, our layout tools, and our design rule checkers. Today we're limited by the number of engineers. Tomorrow, those engineers are going to be supported by a bunch of agents. We're going to be exploring the design space like you've never seen before, and we're going to use the tools that we use today. I think tool use is going to cause the software companies to skyrocket. The reason why it hasn't happened yet is because the agents aren't good enough at using their tools yet. Either these companies are going to build the agents themselves, or agents are going to get good enough to be able to use those tools. I think it's going to be a combination of both. One interpretation is that Nvidia's moat is really that you've locked up many years of these scarce components. It's one of the things that we can do that is hard for someone else to do. We've made enormous commitments upstream. Some of it is explicit, these commitments that you mentioned. Some of it is implicit. As a result of that process of informing, inspiring, and aligning with CEOs of all different industries upstream, they're willing to make the investments. The fact is that Nvidia's downstream supply chain and our downstream demand is so large, they're willing to make the investment upstream. Tesla sold extremely good electric vehicles to China for a long time. iPhones are sold in China, extremely good. They didn't cause them lock-in. China will still make their version of EVs and they're dominating. Their smartphones are dominating. When we started the conversation today, you acknowledged that Nvidia's position is very different. You used words like moat. The single most important thing to our company is the richness of our ecosystem, which is about developers. 50% of the AI developers are in China. The United States should not give that up. But we have a lot of Nvidia developers in the US, and that doesn't prevent American labs from also being able to use other accelerators in the future. The premise that even if we competed in China, that we're going to lose that market anyways… You're not talking to somebody who woke up a loser. That loser attitude, that loser premise makes no sense to me. We're not a car. We are not a car. The fact that I can buy this car brand one day and use another car brand another day, easy. Computing is not like that. There's a reason why the x86 deal exists. There's a reason why ARM is so sticky. These ecosystems are hard to replace. It costs an enormous amount of time and energy, and most people don't want to do it. So it's our job to continue to nurture that ecosystem, to keep advancing the technology so that we can compete in the marketplace. Conceding a marketplace based on the premise you described, I simply can't acknowledge that. It makes no sense. Because I don't think the United States is a loser. Our industry is not a loser. That losing proposition, that losing mindset, makes no sense to me. Okay. I'll move on. I just want to make sure that— You don't have to move on. I'm enjoying it. Okay, great. Then I won't. I appreciate that. But I think maybe the crux… and thanks for walking around the circles with me, because I think it helps bring out what the crux here is. The crux is you're going to extremes. Your argument starts from extremes: that if we give them any compute at all in this narrow moment, we will lose everything. No, I think what my argument is— Those extremes, they're childish. Let me just make my argument for myself. The idea is not that there is some key threshold of compute. It's that any marginal compute is helpful. So if you have more compute, you can train a better model. And I just want you to acknowledge that any marginal sales for the American technology industry is beneficial. I actually don't… If the AI models that run on those chips are capable of cyber offensive capabilities, or the chips are training models with cyber capabilities and running more instances of those models, it is not a nuclear weapon, but it enables a weapon of a kind. The logic that you use, you might as well say it to microprocessors and DRAMs. You might as well say it to electricity. But in fact we do have export controls on the technology that is relevant to making the most advanced DRAM. We have all kinds of export controls on China for all kinds of chip-making stuff. We sell a lot of DRAM and CPUs into China, and I think it's right. I guess this goes back to the fundamental question of, is AI different? If you have the kind of technology where they can find these zero-days in software, is that something where we want to minimize China's ability to get there first, to deploy it widely? We want the United States to be ahead. We can control that. How do we control that if the chips are already there and they're using them to train that model? We have tons of compute. We have tons of AI researchers. We're racing as fast as we can. Again, we have more nuclear weapons than anybody else, but we don't want to send enriched uranium anywhere. We're not enriched uranium. It's a chip, and it's a chip that they can make themselves. But there's a reason they're buying it from you. We have quotes from the founders of Chinese companies that say that they’re bottlenecked on compute. Because our chips are better. On balance, our chips are better. There's just no question about it. In the absence of our chip… Can you acknowledge that Huawei had a record year? Can you acknowledge that a whole bunch of chip companies have gone public? Can you acknowledge that? Yes. Can you also acknowledge that we used to have a very large share in that market, and we no longer have a large share in that market? We can also acknowledge that China is about 40% of the world's technology industry. To concede that market for the United States technology industry is a disservice to our country. It is a disservice to our national security. It is a disservice to our technology leadership, all for the benefit of one company. It makes no sense to me. I guess I'm confused. It feels like you're making two different statements. One is that we're going to win this competition with Huawei because our chips are going to be way better if we're allowed to compete. Another is that they would be doing the same exact thing without us anyway. How can both of those things be true at the same time? It's obviously true. In the absence of a better choice, you'll take the only choice you have. How is that illogical? It's so logical. The reason they want Nvidia chips is that they're better. Yeah. Better is more compute. More compute means you can train a better model. No, it's just better. It's better because it's easier to program. We have a better ecosystem. But whatever the better is, whatever the better is… And of course we're going to send them compute. So what? The fact of the matter is that we get to benefit. Don't forget, we get the benefit of American technology leadership. We get the benefit of developers working on the American tech stack. Now, the policies that you're advocating resulted in the American telecommunications industry being policied out of basically the world, to the point where we don't control our own telecommunications anymore. Okay, let's just step back. It seems like the crux here is there's a potential benefit and there's a potential cost. What we're trying to figure out is, is the benefit worth the cost? I guess I'm trying to get you to acknowledge the potential cost. Compute is an input to training powerful models. Powerful models do have powerful offensive capabilities, like cyber attacks. Fact. All five layers of the tech stack for AI are important. The United States ought to go win all five of them. They're all important. The one that is the most important, of course, is the AI application layer. The layer that diffuses into society, the one that uses it most will benefit from this industrial revolution most. But my point is that every layer has to succeed. Today you're talking about Mythos because Mythos is important. Sure. That's fantastic. I will tell you exactly about today's conversation, about how your policy and what you imagined literally caused the United States to concede the second largest market in the world for no good reason at all. We shouldn't concede it. If we lose it, we lose it. But why do we concede it? Now nobody is advocating an all or nothing. Nobody's advocating all or nothing, meaning we ship everything to China at all times. Nobody's advocating that. We should always have the best technology here. We should always have the most technology here, and the first. But we should also try to compete and win around the world. Both of those things can simultaneously happen. It requires some amount of nuance, some amount of maturity instead of absolutes. The world is just not absolutes. Okay. The argument hinges on this. They've built models that are specified for the best chips that they make in a few years. Those chips get exported around the world. That sets the standard. Because of EUV export controls, as we said, you're going to move on to 1.6nm. They're still going to be on 7nm, even after a few years from now. It may make sense that domestically they would prefer, "Hey, we've got so much energy, we can manufacture at scale. We'll still keep using 7nm." But on the exporting thing, their 7nm chips have to be competitive against your 1.6nm chips. Is Blackwell 50 times more advanced lithography than Hopper? Is it 50 times? Not even close. I just kept saying it over and over again. Moore's Law is dead. Between Hopper and Blackwell, from the transistors themselves, call it 75%. It was three years apart, 75%. Blackwell is 50 times Hopper. My point is, architecture matters. Computer science matters. Semiconductor physics matters as well, but computer science matters. The impact of AI largely comes from the computing stack, which is the reason why CUDA is so effective, which is the reason why CUDA is so beloved. It's an ecosystem, a computing architecture that allows for so much flexibility that if you wanted to change an architecture completely—create something like MoE, create something like diffusion, create something that's disaggregated—you could do so. It's easy to do. So the fact of the matter is, AI is about the stack above as much as it is about the architecture below. To the extent that we have architectures and software stacks that are optimized for our stack, for our ecosystem, it is obviously good, because we started the conversation today about how Nvidia's ecosystem is so rich. Why do people always love programming CUDA first? They do. They do. So do the researchers in China. But if we are forced to leave China, if we're forced to leave China, first of all, it's a policy mistake. Obviously it has backlash. It has turned out badly for the United States. It enabled, it accelerated their chip industry. It forced all of their AI ecosystem to focus on their internal architectures. It's not too late, but nonetheless it has already happened. You're going to see in the future, they're not stuck at 7nm, obviously. They're good at manufacturing. They will continue to advance from 7nm and beyond. Now, is there a 10x difference between 5nm and 7nm? The answer is no. Architecture matters. Networking matters. That's why Nvidia bought Mellanox. Networking matters. Energy matters. So all of that stuff matters. It's not simplistic, like the way you're trying to distill it. We can move on from China, but that actually raises an interesting question. We were discussing earlier these bottlenecks at TSMC and memory and so forth. So if we're in this world where you're already the majority of N3—and at some point you'll be N2 and you'll be a majority of that—do you see that you could go back to N7, the spare capacity at an older process node, and say, "Hey, the demand for AI is so great and our capacity to expand the leading edge is not meeting it, so we're going to make a Hopper or Ampere, but with everything we know about numerics today and all the other improvements you described"? Do you see that world happening before 2030? It's not necessary to. The reason for that is because with every generation, the architecture is more than just the transistor scale. You're doing so much engineering and packaging and stacking, and the numerics and the system architecture. When you run out of capacity, to easily go back to another node… That's a level of R&D that no one could afford. We could afford to lean forward. I don't think we could afford to go back. Now, if the world simply says… If on that day, let's do the thought experiment, on that day we go, "Listen, we're just never going to have more capacity ever again." Would I go back and use 7nm? In a heartbeat, of course I would. One question somebody I was talking to had is, why doesn't Nvidia run multiple different chip projects at the same time with totally different architecture? So you could do something like a Cerebras-style wafer scale. You could do a Dojo-style huge package. You could do one without CUDA. You have the resources and the engineering talent to do all of these in parallel. So why put all the eggs in one basket, given who knows where AI might go and architectures might go? Oh, we could. It's just that we don't have a better idea. We could do all of those things. It's just not better. We simulate it all in our simulator, provably worse. So we wouldn't do it. We're working on exactly the projects that we want to work on. If the workload were to change dramatically—and I don't mean the algorithms, I actually mean the workload, and that depends on the shape of the market—we may decide to add other accelerators. For example, recently we added Groq, and we're going to fold Groq into our CUDA ecosystem. We're doing that now because the value of tokens has gone up so high that you could have different pricing of tokens. Back in the old days, just a couple years ago, tokens were either free or barely expensive. But now you can have different customers, and those customers want different answers. Because the customers make so much money—for example, our software engineers—if I can give them much more responsive tokens so that they're even more productive than they are today, I would pay for it. But that market has only recently emerged. So I think we now have the ability to have the same model, based on the response time, have different segments. That's the reason why we decided to expand the Pareto frontier and create a segment of inference that is faster response time, even though it's lower throughput. Until now, higher throughput is always better. We think there could be a world where there could be very high ASP tokens, and even though the throughput is lower in the factory, the ASPs make up for it. That's the reason why we did it. But otherwise, from an architecture perspective, if I had more money, I would put more behind Nvidia’s architecture. I think this idea of extremely premium tokens and just the disaggregation of the inference market is very interesting… the segmentation of it. Yeah. Alright, final question. Suppose the deep learning revolution didn't happen. What would Nvidia be doing? Obviously games, but given— Accelerated computing, the same thing we've been doing all along. The premise of our company is that Moore's law is going to… General purpose computing is good for a lot of things, but for a lot of computation it's not ideal. So we combined an architecture called a GPU, CUDA, to a CPU, so that we can accelerate the workload of the CPU. Different kernels of code or algorithms could be offloaded onto our GPU. As a result, you speed up an application by 100x, 200x. Where can you use that? Obviously engineering and science and physics, data processing, computer graphics, image generation, all kinds of things. Even if AI doesn't exist today, Nvidia would be very, very large. The reason for that is fairly fundamental, which is that the ability for general purpose computing to continue to scale has largely run its course. And the only way… Not the only way, but the way to do that is through domain-specific acceleration. One of the domains that we started with was computer graphics, but there are many other domains. There's all kinds. Particle physics and fluids, structured data processing, all kinds of different types of algorithms that benefit from CUDA. Our mission was really to bring accelerated computing to the world and advance the type of applications that general purpose computing can't do, and scale to the level of capability that helps break through certain fields of science. Some of the early applications were molecular dynamics, seismic processing for energy discovery, image processing of course, all of those kinds of fields where general purpose computing is just simply too inefficient to do so. If there were no AI, I would be very sad. But because of the advances that we made in computing, we democratized deep learning. We made it possible for any researcher, any scientist, anywhere, any student, to be able to access a PC or a GeForce add-in card and do amazing science. That fundamental promise hasn't changed, not even a little bit. If you watch GTC, there's the whole beginning part of it. None of it's AI. That whole part of it with computational lithography or our quantum chemistry work, data processing work, all of that stuff is unrelated to AI. And it's still very important. I know that AI is very interesting and quite exciting, but there's a lot of people doing a lot of very important work that's not AI related, and tensors are not the only way that you compute it. We want to help everybody. Jensen, thank you so much. You're welcome. I enjoyed it. Me too.

JUST IN: Secretary Of Agriculture Brooke Rollins Hosts Other Cabinet Members For Press Conference

Forbes Breaking News(5.5M) ·43:13 ·834 views · 2026-04-15
Brooke Rollins (Secretary of Agriculture) Howard Lutnick (Secretary of Commerce) Kevin Hassett (Director, National Economic Council)
The Trump administration marked Tax Day by highlighting the "Working Families Tax Cut Act," which delivers historic relief to middle-class Americans and rural communities, while simultaneously announcing the launch of the first-ever USDA Office of Se...
TL;DR The Trump administration marked Tax Day by highlighting the "Working Families Tax Cut Act," which delivers historic relief to middle-class Americans and rural communities, while simultaneously announcing the launch of the first-ever USDA Office of Seafood to integrate...

TL;DR

The Trump administration marked Tax Day by highlighting the "Working Families Tax Cut Act," which delivers historic relief to middle-class Americans and rural communities, while simultaneously announcing the launch of the first-ever USDA Office of Seafood to integrate fishermen into federal agricultural support programs.

Economic Relief and Tax Implementation

  • Broad Tax Savings: The administration reported that 53 million filers have claimed at least one new tax cut, with the average refund this season exceeding $3,400.
  • Specific Provisions:
      • No Tax on Tips/Overtime: Over 6 million filers claimed "no tax on tips," and 25 million claimed "no tax on overtime," resulting in average savings of $7,000 for service workers.
      • Senior Relief: 51 million seniors (88% of those on Social Security) will now pay no tax on their Social Security benefits.
      • Standard Deduction: Over 100 million filers utilized the now-permanent double standard deduction.
  • Capital Investment: The act made permanent 100% immediate expensing for capital-intensive business investments, specifically aiding farmers and small businesses.

Revitalizing Rural America and Agriculture

  • The "Death Tax": Secretary Brooke Rollins confirmed the act averted the sunset of the death tax exemption, protecting two million family farms from liquidation.
  • Direct Rural Investment:
      • Rural Opportunity Zones: $100 billion is being driven into rural investment through Opportunity Zones, shifting the program's focus from urban to heartland communities.
      • Health Transformation: $50 billion is committed to the Rural Health Transformation program, overseen by Dr. Oz.
  • Commodity Price Protection: Reference prices for major commodities (corn, soybeans, wheat) will increase by 10% to 21%, providing up to $13 billion in additional revenue protection starting this October.
  • Trade Deficit Reduction: The administration reported cutting the inherited $50 billion agricultural trade deficit by 40% through 18 new trade deals.

The Historic Launch of the USDA Office of Seafood

  • "Farmers of the Sea": Secretary Rollins announced the first-ever USDA Office of Seafood, officially recognizing commercial fishermen as food producers rather than just resource harvesters.
  • Integration of Services: The office will provide fishermen access to USDA tools previously reserved for land farmers, including:
      • Risk management and insurance tools.
      • Marketing support and federal financing.
      • Disaster and weather incident relief.
  • Regulatory and Economic Protection:
      • Inspection Standards: Officials noted that currently, only 0.1% of imported seafood is inspected; the new office aims to protect domestic markets from "toxic" imported fish.
      • Defunding Offshore Wind: Fourth-generation lobsterman Dustin Delano praised the administration for eliminating subsidies for offshore wind projects that threaten traditional fishing grounds.

Federal Efficiency and Regulatory Reform

  • Permitting Acceleration: Secretary Doug Burgum highlighted a massive reduction in bureaucracy, reporting that Environmental Impact Statements (EIS) that previously took four years are now being completed in 24 days while still following the law.
  • Eliminating "Bloat": Secretary Rollins identified several taxpayer-funded studies (e.g., studies on transgender mice and "racism in the pest business") that were defunded to refocus the USDA on core agricultural production.
  • Energy Independence and Inputs:
      • Fertilizer Crisis: Secretary Rollins reported that fertilizer prices have risen 40% in the last month; the administration is engaging DOJ and fertilizer CEOs to prevent price gouging.
      • Natural Gas Advantage: Secretary Burgum noted that because the U.S. is a net exporter of natural gas (the primary component of nitrogen fertilizer), the administration intends to build domestic infrastructure to ensure American farmers have the world’s lowest fertilizer costs.
"For the first time in a long time, commercial fishermen are being recognized for what we truly are: food producers... This is not just about policy; it’s about people. It’s about ensuring that the American fisherman, the farmer of the sea, has a future in this country."

— Dustin Delano, *Maine Lobsterman and Fisherman in Residence at NOAA*

"We’ve taken federal employees and we’ve basically created mind-numbing, soul-sucking jobs where all they do is follow a bunch of regulations that aren’t literally something that Congress passed... When you get rid of the red tape, it not only helps the industry, it helps the federal team members."

— Doug Burgum, *Secretary of the Interior*

"We have a handful of companies in the world supplying fertilizer... I will not leave this building in two years and nine months if we have not made significant progress to build out that infrastructure [in America]."

— Brooke Rollins, *Secretary of Agriculture*

He shared the story, we've all heard it, but he was talking to a person that was waiting tables at a restaurant he was at, asking about what he could do when he was campaigning. They said, "I have to pay taxes on my hard-earned tips." He came up with this idea, drove it through, and got it passed. A sampling of this is going out right now. People that work professionally or part-time are seeing anywhere between $3,000 and $10,000 of savings of their hard-earned money staying in their own pockets. The economists are saying on average it's about $7,000. What a gift to America for people that work hard. And then there is the no tax on overtime. Many of you, I'm sure, did what I did when I was a kid. I couldn't wait to get past 40 hours because that's when you finally started making a little bit of money getting time and a half. Now, to have no tax on overtime, the incentive for people to work harder begins their own steps towards the American dream. These are common sense policies. In terms of gratitude, I want to say thank you to President Trump and thank you to the fellow cabinet secretaries like Brooke Rollins that are here today. We're working for a President that works 20 hours a day, getting up every day fighting for the American people. It's so inspiring and so motivating to see the policies actually in action coming together. As it relates to the fishermen that are up here, people are like, why is the Secretary of Interior talking about oceans? But let's all remember a couple things. Between agriculture and interior, we have 700 million acres of land and 700 million acres of subsurface. You refused to let this industry be forgotten. So yes, today is a milestone, but it's also a starting point.
