Hans Ulrich Obrist: What business can learn from the art world | Podcast | In Good Company

Norges Bank Investment Management(105.0K) ·56:56 ·1.5K views · 2026-04-15
Nicolai Tangen (CEO, Norges Bank Investment Management) Hans Ulrich Obrist (Artistic Director, Serpentine Galleries)
The most effective leaders act as "junction makers," fostering innovation by creating unexpected connections between disciplines, listening deeply to practitioners, and embracing serendipity over rigid master plans.
TL;DR The most effective leaders act as "junction makers," fostering innovation by creating unexpected connections between disciplines, listening deeply to practitioners, and embracing serendipity over rigid master plans. Defining the Role: Hans Ulrich Obrist describes curation...

TL;DR

The most effective leaders act as "junction makers," fostering innovation by creating unexpected connections between disciplines, listening deeply to practitioners, and embracing serendipity over rigid master plans.

The Curator as "Junction Maker" and Enabler

  • Defining the Role: Hans Ulrich Obrist describes curation not as gatekeeping, but as being a "junction maker"—creating situations and unexpected connections between people, ideas, and artworks.
  • The Power of Listening: Creativity begins with listening rather than imposing a framework.
      • Artist Advocacy: Obrist views his role as an "enabler" who helps artists realize dreams that the traditional art world structure might otherwise prohibit.
      • The Studio Visit: Obrist has conducted daily studio visits since 1986, emphasizing that the best insights come from long-term, direct engagement with creators.
  • The "Unrealized Project" Inquiry: A core part of Obrist’s methodology is asking every collaborator about their unrealized projects.
      • Categories of Failure: Projects often remain unrealized because they are too big, too time-intensive, censored, or "self-censored" (ideas the creator didn't dare to try).
      • Untapped Potential: These "dreams" often sit outside an artist's daily routine and represent their most innovative thinking.

Innovation Through Multisensory and Digital Experiences

  • Extending Engagement: To move beyond the "few seconds" visitors typically spend in front of an artwork, the Serpentine creates multisensory environments.
      • Example: A Peter Doig exhibition transformed a gallery into a "listening space," encouraging visitors to stay for hours and return repeatedly.
  • Technology as a Lead Medium: The Serpentine established a dedicated technology department to produce "Future Art Ecosystems."
      • Gaming and Scale: A collaboration with *Fortnite* for a KAWS exhibition attracted 150 million virtual visitors in two weeks, successfully bringing a younger generation into the physical gallery.
      • Ethical AI: Artists like Holly Herndon and Refik Anadol are used to make "invisible" AI datasets visible and audible, demonstrating how data can be sourced and utilized ethically.
  • Bridging Disciplines: The Serpentine Pavilion project (now in its 26th year) invites international architects who have never built in the UK to create temporary structures, blending the discourses of art and architecture.

Long-Term Thinking vs. Short-Termism

  • Continuous Evolution: Obrist argues against the "short-termist deadlines" of the modern world.
      • The "do it" Project: An exhibition based on artist-written instructions that has been evolving and learning for 33 years.
      • Institutional Re-invention: The Serpentine adds new departments (e.g., Ecology, Technology) to its organizational "organigram" to stay relevant to societal shifts.
  • Mondialité: Following the philosophy of Édouard Glissant, Obrist advocates for "mondialité"—a global dialogue that resists the homogenizing forces of globalization while remaining deeply anchored in local culture.
  • The Polyphony of Centers: The art world has moved from a single center (Paris or New York) to a "polyphony" of global hubs, fueled by private foundations and artist-run initiatives.

Business Lessons from the Art World

  • The Art of the Pivot: Artists excel at navigating the "unknowable" and pivoting when faced with uncertainty—skills that are increasingly critical for modern CEOs.
  • Artists in the Boardroom: Obrist advocates for "Artist Placement Groups," suggesting that every corporation or government entity should have an artist-in-residence or an artist on the board to challenge traditional thinking.
  • Extreme Productivity and Rituals: Obrist shares his unconventional methods for managing a global workload:
      • The Night Producer: He employs a "night assistant" to handle correspondence and editing while he sleeps, ensuring a 24-hour productive cycle.
      • The Brutally Early Club: A ritual started in London to hold improvised meetings at 6:30 AM to bypass the rigid scheduling of the business world.

Notable Quotes

"Basically what you're doing is you're a junction maker. You create unexpected connections... using the exhibition as a kind of a medium to create experiences." — Hans Ulrich Obrist (quoting J.G. Ballard)
"The way artists work with the unknowable, the way artists work also with questions, often more than with answers, is something I think which can be very relevant today [for business]." — Hans Ulrich Obrist
"We live in a world which is increasingly polarized and separated... I think it's super important that we have situations where we can bring people together. And I think that's what exhibitions can do." — Hans Ulrich Obrist
"I think that's what the best artists do. They kind of negotiate ways of working which are enabling a global dialogue but at the same time are deeply locally anchored." — Hans Ulrich Obrist
Hi everyone and welcome to In Good Company. I'm Nicolai Tangen and I am the CEO of the Norwegian Sovereign Wealth Fund. And today I'm joined by somebody who I think probably is the world's greatest connector of ideas, people, and art. Hans Ulrich Obrist is the Artistic Director of the Serpentine Galleries in London and widely considered the best art curator in the world. You've curated unbelievable amounts of exhibitions and today we're going to tease out the reasons behind your success, creativity, how to connect with people, and what business can learn from all the kind of things you've done. So a warm welcome. Thank you. Very nice to see you. What does an art curator actually do? Yeah, I've always followed this definition the writer J.G. Ballard gave me when he didn't really understand what my profession was. So I was describing it to him and he said, "So basically what you're doing is you're a junction maker. You create unexpected connections." And I think that's kind of what is at the core of my practice. It's bringing artworks together, bringing people together, creating situations, and using also the exhibition as a kind of a medium to create experiences. What's the key to creating interesting experiences? I mean, I think the first, probably most important step is to listen. The poet Etel Adnan said once that we sort of need to learn to listen again. And I think listening to artists is always the beginning. So I would go to studios. It's something I've been doing since I was in my teens, when I was like 16 or 17. I traveled all over Europe by night train and I went to see artists from Gerhard Richter to Rosemarie Trockel to Louise Bourgeois to Annette Messager to Fischli Weiss. So I saw artists all over Europe initially and then also outside Europe and would just listen to them. And of course, I would also find out what they would like to do. Rather than to somehow squeeze their practice into a kind of a framework, it's to basically listen to what they've maybe not been able to do in the context of the art world. So we see how we can basically change the art world or shift it slightly to make these artists' dreams become a reality. So in a way, to come back to your initial question of what a curator is, I think it's also about enabling and about making things possible. Now, you put together things which weren't meant to be together often. What's the key to getting diverse things to play together? I mean, I think the key is to not stop the serendipity, because very often it's a chain reaction. And it's not necessarily a master plan. I think very often an exhibition has a checklist early on, and one would say that's what we're going to do. It's very limited. I think we go on a journey, we go into the unknowable, and on this path, you know, we have to be open to many different surprises. One of my bigger projects of the last years has been my kind of handwriting project. And that is an example for that, these two little books and my Instagram account. I was with Umberto Eco, the writer and scholar, and he was very old, and he said, basically, "We need to do something to save handwriting." I kind of left his apartment with this task somehow. I wasn't, of course, qualified to do that because I'm not a calligrapher, nor could I found a calligraphy school. Then, in a way, I was with the poet Etel Adnan, whom I quoted earlier, who said that we have to learn to listen again. She also told me that we shouldn't forget that handwriting matters. She started once when we were in a cafe to write a very beautiful poem in her notebook. And suddenly I realized I am with artists, with poets, with writers, with architects every day. I could just ask them to write something and give my social media a mission, no? Because in a way, I didn't really know what to do with Instagram and with social media. I knew that I didn't want it to be about selfies. I knew I didn't want it to be about my food. I didn't want it to be about my travels; it needed to have a mission, a purpose. And then I thought, "Wow, so I could actually do what Umberto Eco told me." And so, in a way, it's the serendipity which leads to a project. I think it's to be open and to be curious and let things flow. Not many people actively believe in serendipity. I kind of love it because it brings out surprising things. What is your relationship to serendipity? I think, in a way, the idea of an encounter with artists can lead us somewhere where we would never have thought we could go. I think that's what very often is the serendipity of such projects. But it's also, I think, the idea of going beyond short-termism. I think we live in a world which is very much sort of ruled by short-termist deadlines, where for a certain moment or up to a certain moment something has to happen. That's, of course, true in every field. It's also true in my field. The exhibition has to open. But I think very often it's interesting that we go on a journey and that an exhibition has a longer life than that. Some of my exhibitions have been evolving for more than 30 years. We started a project called "do it," where I ask artists to write instructions and recipes, and basically people, museums, and people at home everywhere in the world can interpret and can basically do the artwork themselves. That project has been on the road for, yeah, 33 years now, and it's never stopped. There has always been a new version. It keeps learning, it keeps evolving. I think the idea also that exhibitions in a way don't start from this idea that they know everything, that we kind of set up vehicles or structures which can learn, is very important. What's the recipe for a great exhibition? I mean, I don't think that there is one recipe. I think that in a way an exhibition needs to come up with new rules of the game. It needs to come up with new connections and new encounters, and it needs to create an experience we haven't had before. Very often it's also bringing disciplines together. For example, we've just had an exhibition at Serpentine of Peter Doig, and talking to the artist, we figured out that his desire is really to bring the world of art, painting, and music together. Little by little we realized that it also includes poetry, because he collaborated with poets. So we created an exhibition and invited him to basically create an exhibition at Serpentine where on the one hand he presented new paintings. At the same time, he transformed the exhibition space into a listening space. People could sit down, they could have a chat, they could have a conversation, they could also lie down and just chill, and they could listen to his music. They could listen to the music he has basically in his studio and plays in his studio. But they could also come for special events. There were these basically sessions every Sunday. So it means that people came actually not only once to view the exhibition, but all of a sudden you had people who came every Sunday, or who came every day to listen to new music. In a way it created a completely new experience. And also in a way it binds people to spend more time. Because on average people spend very little time in front of an artwork in a museum—probably a few seconds, sometimes longer. But I think it's interesting when we can create an experience. Are you fast when you go through exhibitions? Yeah. Because I'm really fast. I'm fast until I'm not. If all of a sudden I'm fascinated, then I can spend a lot of time. So it's kind of both. I think it's interesting that we create multisensory experiences where actually visitors can spend more time, as they did, you know, in Peter Doig's exhibition. What's your imprint on this? Where can we see Obrist in your exhibitions? I mean, in terms of solo exhibitions, when we do solo exhibitions at Serpentine, we most of the time invite artists to take over the space and create their world and show their work as a kind of a world-building. Then I think it's an enabling. It's making that possible. It's of course very different with group shows. With group shows, the curator kind of defines in dialogue with the artists the rule of the game. We're working right now with my colleague Ben Vickers and Soundwalk Collective on the Vatican Pavilion, the Holy See Pavilion for the Venice Biennale, which is going to open next month. There we basically came up with this idea of this interdisciplinary figure, the saint Hildegard von Bingen, who lived in the 11th century. So many musicians and artists today are inspired by her composing, by her music, by her writing, and by the way she connected also to nature and healing, because she was also a healer. So we decided, in dialogue with artists, to sort of do a garden, a sound garden in Venice. It's a hidden garden near the railway station. And this garden is basically going to be accessible for the exhibition. Visitors will come there and listen to sound interpretations of 21st-century artists from Blood Orange to Patti Smith to Bonga Macanga. Many different artists will interpret Hildegard von Bingen's work for today. So here we would define a rule of the game in which then the different artists can intervene. It depends a lot if it's a solo show or a group show. Tell me about the importance of multisensory exhibitions. What does it do to the visitor? You mean when an exhibition addresses multiple senses? Well, yeah, what happens? How do you think about it? I mean, how many senses do you want to engage? And is more senses better? I mean, I think we can never say an exhibition has to be this or that way, because each time it's a different situation. But I think if we want to bind the viewer, and if we want the viewer to spend more time in an exhibition, it's important to appeal to multiple senses, definitely. And I think that's something, I mean, the example of Peter Doig is a case in point. People spend hours and they kept coming back. And that's also what's happening, for example, with our experiments with technology. When I joined the Serpentine in 2006, we started to think about what could be new departments, and we started to basically introduce an ecological department, so to say, where we looked at the environmental aspects of what the museum does. But we also felt it would be important to kind of connect to technology, because around 2012, 2013, most of the museum work with technology happened maybe on the website, but it wasn't really central in museums. It was clear that it would become a very important dimension of art of the future. So we started new experiments in art and technology, and many of these exhibitions are very multisensory. We created, for example, exhibitions with video games, because today more than three billion people play video games. What was a niche medium has become one of the lead mediums of our time. Many visual artists are engaging with that medium. So we created kind of mixed reality installations, physical installations by artists with video game components. And that again—exhibitions, for example, of Gabriel Massan and also Danielle Brathwaite-Shirley and others—these exhibitions really bind the viewer and visitors spend a lot of time. It also brought in a completely new generation of visitors who otherwise wouldn't visit the museum. So we also kind of think in relation to that, it's interesting to create new alliances, to kind of connect, because obviously museums always work with other museums, but we think it's interesting to create new alliances also with brands and with companies in technology. For example, for the KAWS exhibition, we partnered up with Fortnite, and that partnership meant that all of a sudden Serpentine was on the landing page of Fortnite. We had 150 million visitors on that space in two weeks, which is more than any exhibition we've ever done. And that brought us a lot of visitors into the physical space. The same exhibition basically happened in the virtual space on Fortnite and in the physical space. The physical paintings were actually in the gallery. And all of a sudden, you know, teenagers brought their parents to the Serpentine, which in a way otherwise would be the other way around. The same thing happened with K-pop. We did a whole collaboration with BTS and K-pop and the young artist Jakob Kudsk Steensen. And all of a sudden the world of contemporary art and the world of K-pop came together. Yeah. Are the best artists also the most technically accomplished using modern technology in a better way? Is there a correlation there? I think that, I remember I saw a Hockney exhibition where he had drawn on iPads, right? You remember that probably from the Royal Academy. Yeah. At the moment we actually show an exhibition of David Hockney at Serpentine, which just opened. It's there until early September or late August. He has always gone beyond paintings. He's a painter who always had the curiosity to go into other fields. So he wrote "Secret Knowledge", which is a book. At the same time he worked with opera; he worked with opera sets and theater sets. And then he always came back to painting. I would say already in the '80s he was very fascinated by technology, but it was too slow for him. Drawing was still faster. But then around 2006, 7, 8, 9—I don't remember exactly the year, but about approximately 20 years ago or 18 years ago—he started to all of a sudden really experiment more with technology. And then he started to make these extraordinary works on the iPad. Initially it was flowers and then landscapes. It really intensified when, during the lockdown, he was in Normandy in France, and he created this amazing work, "A Year in Normandy." And it's of course a very physical, deeply embodied piece because it's printed. It fills the gallery; it's a panorama. It's in a way an homage to Bayeux, one can say, as it's inspired by the tapestry of Bayeux. So it's interesting because later this year, tomorrow, I'm going to the British Museum. He's showing in the British Museum. Our show is basically ending when that starts. So it's kind of a segue almost. And for Hockney, it was very inspiring to visit the tapestry of Bayeux. He created hundreds of works outdoors. Bit like Monet, the impressionists would paint plein air. Hockney was plein air, but he wasn't there with the easel and the paint. He was there with his iPad. And he was basically painting. And then he combined about a hundred of these works into one very long work, which is inspired by Bayeux, and which is basically going around the corners of the space, so one has to walk in order to experience it. Then at the same time, you know, he would return again to painting as he always did. And in the two powder rooms in Serpentine North, the two central spaces, there are two new paintings. So yeah, that's definitely a very physical experience, but it's been created using technology. And it took him a long time to basically learn how to use that tool. What's going to be the function of AI in art? I think it's interesting right now to see how many artists are working with AI. And we started of course, when we 12 years ago began these experiments in art and technology at Serpentine, we quite early on also focused on AI. Last year we had an exhibition of Holly Herndon and Mat Dryhurst, and they worked with choirs all over the UK. They created a book of hymns, and this is the book of hymns basically, which then was interpreted by these different choirs. And the choirs forming these recordings are forming a dataset. Visitors could actually experience this dataset. Because normally one wouldn't see and understand how a dataset actually works. But does it matter for you whether art is created by AI or people? I think it's always both. In the case of Holly Herndon and Mat Dryhurst, the artists worked with people, and it was a collaborative situation with AI. And what was interesting is that Paul Klee once said that art makes the invisible visible. And in a way a lot of AI functions we use, we don't really understand, and don't really know; they're kind of opaque. We don't really understand how it works. And they really wanted to make the process of forming a dataset visible and audible for the viewer. And then I think at the same time choirs are a very old coordination technology, no? And AI is a form of coordination technology. So they created that connection. And interestingly enough, it was for the viewer or for the visitor who came to Serpentine and experienced this AI work, "The Call" of Holly Herndon and Mat Dryhurst, also a way to understand how actually artists can use a dataset and AI ethically. The same thing also happened with Refik Anadol. I mean, Refik Anadol created this very immersive installation in close collaborations, of course, also with Google. He's been collaborating with them for a long time, and with Nvidia. And he created very big datasets, but all ethically sourced—Smithsonian, from National Geographic. So I think it's interesting also how artists can set an example of how AI datasets can be ethically sourced. Let's talk about relationships. What is key to establishing good relationships with artists? I think relationships with artists are about liberating time. I think it's something which always begins with a studio visit. It begins with a long conversation. So I think it's something which has to do also with attention. And you visit them typically? In their galleries. Yeah, I would make studio visits. Some artists don't have a physical studio, then we meet in a cafe, but that's also a studio visit. Yeah, I do that. I do that every day. How many artists have you visited? I mean, I've been doing it since 1986, because I started when I was a teenager, so it's been 40 years. So if you would think that on average I visit a studio a day, it's 40 times 365. It's a lot. You also record some of your conversations. Yeah, I started at a certain moment in the early '90s to record them. Initially because I didn't really remember everything artists told me, but also because I started to think that maybe it could be interesting for the future as a document to have the voices recorded. I basically was very inspired by Vasari, and how Vasari wrote "The Lives of the Artists" and wrote "The Lives of the Architects." So this was during the Renaissance? Yeah, the Renaissance. And then more recently, David Sylvester, who in the 20th century would talk again and again to artists like Philip Guston or Francis Bacon, as a matter of fact. He did a whole book of his conversations with Francis Bacon. Incredible, incredible book. And it's an incredible book. And I read that book as a teenager. And I started to think, you know, because I have this long-term relationship with artists, that I could maybe do something similar. I could sort of record, and I mean today I have—the archive is about 4,500 hours, so it is a dataset. What are you going to do with it? I don't know yet completely. I mean so far... So that's the most comprehensive collection of interviews in the world, right? Yeah, it's probably, I don't know. But it's definitely also not only very broad, but it's also, you know, deep in the sense that I would talk to the same artists again and again. I have certain artists with whom I have 20, 30, or 40 interviews over the years, because I would interview them every year a couple of times. Do you ask the same questions? Yeah, there's a couple of recurrent questions I always ask. What are they? What are they? So one question I always ask is the question about the unrealized projects, because I do think it's very interesting. I mean I think we all, not only in the art world, we all do have unrealized projects, and it's interesting that it's only in very few professions that actually these unrealized projects are being published. I mean, architects do publish them, because the competition culture in a way, the architectural competition, very often leads to them being published. But we know very little about poets, visual artists, filmmakers. But I suppose also in your field, we at the end of the day know a lot about CEOs and unrealized projects. So I think it's interesting. What can we learn from them? So let's say now we interview a CEO, "Hey, what is your unrealized project?" What do you think we can learn from that concept? I think what we can learn is... Now it's an interesting question of what we can learn from it. But I think in terms of artists, what we can definitely learn is what... Maybe we need to look at the range of the unrealized projects, because I think there are many different types of unrealized projects. There are certain projects which are too big to be realized. There are certain projects which are too time-intense to be realized. There are certain projects which are maybe unrealizable—their utopias, their dreams. There are certain projects which are censored; that can be a reason for it not to be realized. And then of course there are also projects which are self-censored. Doris Lessing, the writer, always said one category not to be underestimated is the self-censorship—projects we would like to do but don't dare to do. And I mean in terms of the art world, very often artists have projects outside their kind of daily routine, because they're basically invited to be in biennials, artists are invited to do gallery shows, they're invited to do museum shows, they're invited to participate in collection exhibitions, but very often they have projects outside these parameters. And these are very often the most interesting projects. And so then we need to think how could we make them happen, no? Of course the obvious follow-up here is, what is your unrealized project? Yeah, so my unrealized project—there's a long list, because I think a lot of exhibitions I wanted to do early on fell through and I still one way or the other would like to do some of them. The Hildegard von Bingen project we are doing now with Ben Vickers and the Soundwalk Collective and these different artists is one of these unrealized projects. We always wanted to do something about Hildegard von Bingen, so that's now happening. I always also felt, having organized many exhibitions, worked in museums, and being the Artistic Director of an institution at Serpentine, I've always been thinking what is missing in the art world. What is missing today is a kind of a new Black Mountain College. What is missing is... What is that? The Black Mountain College was this extraordinary school in the US after the Second World War. Josef Albers taught there, Cage, Buckminster Fuller. It was a very interdisciplinary moment where art, architecture, music, science, it all sort of came together, and I think today there is a real necessity for such a school. I think it's, and that's something we are starting to work on because Marina Paul Jobs basically saved this building, the San Francisco Art Institute. When that ended, you know, there was a very big question of what it would become in the future, and it has this Diego Rivera painting. She decided to actually buy it and then guarantee that it would be a school for the future, and asked Ebit George and me to work with her together on ideas of how such a school could be. And so that's definitely a still unrealized project which now will be realized, which I think can be very exciting. Do you ever think about the fact that you have to sell tickets? I mean we are at the Serpentine, we have free admission. We're in a park, we're in Kensington Gardens, and we want the experience to be for everyone. So we've always had free admissions, but we of course think a lot about visitor figures. We want the experience to be there for as many people as possible. Does that influence how you, what shows you put on? The fact that you need to have visitors. I mean I think the program has to be a mix. I mean for our CEO, for Bettina Korek, for myself, for the teams, what is really important is that we have moments where we can show extremely well-known artists like David Hockney, or last year Peter Doig, or soon to come next year Lisa Brice, alongside younger emerging practitioners. And I think a good program in a museum is a mix of that. It's a mix of new unexpected positions where visitors can basically discover artists they haven't seen before, and at the same time they can see artists maybe they already know, but from a different unexpected angle. So it's about finding the right balance, finding the right mix, no? But also the program, because I think in your questions I really liked that question about what makes a good program, no, and I think what you just—your question leads in a way to that. It's the question of what makes a good program in an institution. I think it's about finding the right mix between very well-known artists whom one shows from an unexpected angle and new artists whom visitors can discover. But then at the same time also if you want to understand the forces which are effective in art, it's important to somehow also understand what's happening in other disciplines. That's also why for us, for example, it's very important that we have this dimension with architecture, no? Because if we look at Vasari in the Renaissance, "Lives of the Artists" and "Lives of the Architects," which is the book which is somehow the reason why I became a curator because as a teenager I would always read it and read it. I was thinking, I mean Vasari's book kind of gave me the idea that one could meet the artists of our time, because that wasn't an obvious idea initially. But all of a sudden I kind of realized there are actually amazing artists out there of our time, and I could go and meet them. And that prompted, when I was 16 or 17, this idea of visiting studios quite feverishly all over Europe. Of course, also having been so inspired by this book means that I always felt that we need in a program in a museum to kind of connect art and architecture. And that's what we do with our Pavilion. We're now in the 26th year, and initially when the program started, when Julia Peyton-Jones began it in 2000 with Zaha Hadid—so the Pavilion program began in the year 2000 with Zaha Hadid. It was really about artists who haven't built in London before, who haven't built in the UK. And that is still the rule of the game, but initially it was very well-known artists, because it's interesting that London is such a global city for many different things—for business, for many art forms—but it's interesting, it has also produced an extraordinary generation or several extraordinary generations of architects. If we think of Zaha Hadid, if we think of Norman Foster, if we think of Richard Rogers, if we think of David Chipperfield, if we think of the Archigram group, if we think of Farshid Moussavi, if we think of Cedric Price. And on and on, right? So it's an amazing architecture scene here, but it was always quite closed for architects from abroad to build. And that's really what the Serpentine Pavilion tried to do from the beginning; it's inviting international architects for the first time to build here, and open the discourse. But then of course at a certain moment, after about 12 years, in 2012—I joined the gallery in 2006, and then around 2012—we realized that the scheme had become very well known, and it would actually be a great opportunity now to open it up for a younger generation of architects. So we invited Sou Fujimoto, who was the first younger architect we invited, who just last year became very well known since and has done the Osaka famous Ring, the biggest wood structure ever built. The year after we invited the Chilean architect Smiljan Radic, who just was announced last month to have won the Pritzker Prize this year. And so that was 12, 13, 14, and so over the last 12, 13, 14 years, we've continued in that direction. We've invited younger emerging practitioners, and that means we could also through that make the architecture discourse more diverse, more open, and invite more female architects. It's fascinating for example that architects like Frida Escobedo or Lina Ghotmeh, whom we invited, now went on to build very big and to win very big competitions. Lina Ghotmeh won the British Museum competition. Frida Escobedo is building the foreign ministry in Qatar and is also building the new Metropolitan wing. Sumayya Vally, who was the youngest architect we've invited—she was 29 when we invited her—is now involved in a very important bridge project. So you were part of, you were basically part of making them famous. Yeah, and in a way it allows us to bring art and architecture together in an institution. And now it allows us also sometimes to have artists who actually have a desire to work with architecture to do the pavilion. For example, Olafur Eliasson or also Theaster Gates were the artists who did the pavilion, or last year Marina Tabassum from Bangladesh, and this year it's a very young office from Mexico, MUDAC. And that happens concurrently to what we do with visual art. And then we do the same thing with poetry. We connect a lot to poetry. We do the same thing also with music, as explained with Peter Doig. So in a way we also believe that in a good program or in a museum program, we need to show visual art in connection to other art forms. Is there a big difference between the various parts of the world, or is art becoming more one global movement? Yeah, that is a very complex question. I think in a way, artists... I mean we live in an age which has more and more homogenizing forces of globalization. And we kind of have a counter-reaction to that which we can see, which are new localisms, which is a lack of tolerance, which are new nationalisms. So I think we've got these two extremes, no? We've got the extreme globalization and the extreme counter-reaction. And I think the most interesting practices right now do what the Martinican philosopher Édouard Glissant, who has always been a big inspiration for me, called "mondialité." They negotiate ways of working which are kind of enabling a global dialogue but at the same time are deeply locally anchored in a way. So they resist the homogenizing forces of globalization, but they also don't fall into this trap that one goes from the local into the localist and no longer is open for a global dialogue. Glissant calls this mondialité. So I think that's what the... You read him every morning, I gather. Yeah, exactly. That's a ritual, one of my rituals, because exactly that's one of the reasons why, because I want to think every morning how we can contribute to this idea of mondialité through what we... Do you read the same pages every day or you read different pages? No, he's got a very big body of work. He's written poems, he's written novels. He's written a very beautiful novel called "Sartorius" about a people whose identity is really defined through relations. And at the same time he's also written on art. It's a very, very big body of work. One can always find new texts. What can business people learn from the art world? I think there are many things. I think of course artists are really good at pivoting. And I think today we live in an environment where it's more and more difficult to predict the future. So I think in a way the way artists work with the unknowable, the way artists work also with questions, often more than with answers, is something I think which can be very relevant today. And I think it's interesting that in the '60s there was this idea of John Latham and Barbara Steveni where they had this idea of APG. They had this idea of an Artist Placement Group, where they thought that every governmental structure but also every company and every corporation should have an artist in residence, and maybe also an artist on the board. And I think the time has come that we should realize that. I think it would be very exciting. Do you ever think about the fact that you have to sell tickets? I mean we are at the Serpentine, we have free admission. We're in a park, we're in Kensington Gardens, and we want the experience to be for everyone. So we've always had free admissions, but we of course think a lot about visitor figures. We want the experience to be there for as many people as possible. Why Cost Per Token Is the Only Metric You Need for AI TCO

NVIDIA(2.2M) ·36:39 ·2.8K views · 2026-04-15
Gerro Prinsloo (NVIDIA)
The traditional focus on hardware-centric metrics like "FLOPs per dollar" fails to capture the true cost of AI; "Cost per Token" is the definitive metric for Total Cost of Ownership (TCO) because it accounts for the entire efficiency stack—from power...
TL;DR The traditional focus on hardware-centric metrics like "FLOPs per dollar" fails to capture the true cost of AI; "Cost per Token" is the definitive metric for Total Cost of Ownership (TCO) because it accounts for the entire efficiency stack—from power generation and cooling...

TL;DR

The traditional focus on hardware-centric metrics like "FLOPs per dollar" fails to capture the true cost of AI; "Cost per Token" is the definitive metric for Total Cost of Ownership (TCO) because it accounts for the entire efficiency stack—from power generation and cooling to software optimization and throughput.

Redefining the Economics of AI Infrastructure

  • The Shift from Inputs to Outputs: Historically, data center efficiency was measured by compute cost or GPU hours. However, as AI data centers evolve into "intelligence factories," the only output that generates revenue or utility is the token.
  • Input vs. Outcome Metrics:
      • GPU Hour: A time-based metric that ignores efficiency, power leakage, and actual work performed.
      • FLOPs per Dollar: Measures raw compute potential but ignores the overhead of the surrounding facility.
      • Cost per Token: A holistic metric that reveals the true margin of an AI business by factoring in both CapEx (hardware/facility) and OpEx (energy/maintenance).
  • Generational Performance Gains: Comparing NVIDIA Blackwell to the previous Hopper generation illustrates the metric gap:
      • FLOPs per dollar shows a ~2x improvement.
      • Cost per million tokens shows a ~35x lower cost.

The Data Center as a "Token Factory"

  • The Atomic Unit of Intelligence: Tokens are the physical goods of the new industrial revolution, with electricity serving as the primary raw material.
  • Factory Economics: Just as a traditional manufacturer must know the cost of every part produced to ensure profitability, AI operators must understand their cost per token. Failing to account for power leaks or low utilization results in "selling at a loss."
  • The "Inference Iceberg": The sticker price of a GPU is only the tip of the iceberg. Beneath the surface lie critical costs including networking, memory, cooling, and—most importantly—power delivery efficiency.
  • Utilization Rates: A factory sitting idle increases the cost per token. Software optimizations like disaggregated inference are essential to keep "token factories" running at maximum capacity.

Power as the Primary Lever for Scalability

  • Power Usage Effectiveness (PUE): Dr. Gerro Prinsloo notes that while hyperscalers achieve ~10% overhead, inefficient data centers can reach 100% overhead (a PUE of 2.0).
      • In a 100% overhead scenario, half of the electricity paid for never reaches the chip to produce a token.
  • Energy Sourcing and Transportation:
      • Generation: Scaling requires a "redundancy of sources," including natural gas, nuclear, geothermal, and emerging "metal fuels."
      • Voltage Optimization: Moving to 800V DC power systems reduces "leaks" during the transport of energy from the grid to the chip.
  • The Cooling Revolution: Shifting from air cooling to liquid-cooled, closed-loop systems (keeping hardware at ~45°C) allows for higher density and more tokens per megawatt without wasting water through evaporation.

Escaping "Spiky" Demand: The Rise of AI Agents

  • From Queries to Continuous Load: Early AI usage was "spiky" (users asking single questions). With the rise of AI agents (e.g., Claude), usage is shifting to a "flat line" where models run 24/7 to complete complex tasks.
  • Unlimited Demand for Intelligence: NVIDIA observes an exponential takeoff in token demand. As businesses deploy an agent for every employee, the pressure on the power grid will move from peak-load management to constant, massive demand.
  • The Power-Compute Convergence: For the first time in history, the utility industry and the data center industry are merging. Utility executives and AI infrastructure planners are now forced to co-design the grid to prevent a global "intelligence shortage."

Notable Quotes

"If you’re running the factory... and you're selling tokens at the same rate as someone else that doesn't have the same amount of losses as you do, your factory's going to go out of business." — Dr. Gerro Prinsloo
"We have fundamentally an unlimited demand for intelligence as a species, and we live in a power-constrained world." — Carter Abdallah
"The atomic unit of the industrial revolution was the electron; in this case, the atomic unit is the token. Tokens can be applied to a wide array of things—medicine, software, entertainment, everything." — Carter Abdallah
"I think that the cost per token really is the metric that we need to talk about here. Anything else is getting lost in either something that is too far up the abstraction ladder or you're getting into the weeds of something that isn't actually the meat that drives your P&L." — Nader Khalil
Okay. Guys, take a second and recognize where we are just in terms of history. For the first time ever, power and compute is coming together. It just happened and it's never happened before, and this is the only first time. And we've got a front row seat. Certain metrics, common metrics that some people use today, for example, would just be looking at the cost per GPU hour or the number of flops per dollar is actually probably not the right metric. Can you explain why something like the cost per token is actually something closer to what you would want to look at? Yeah. I think that's a really good point. The electricity industry, the energy industry kind of went through that same learning. So if I only value the cost, the OpEx side of things—if I said electricity only costs as much as the fuel that I put in plus the people that run the plants and maybe some other operational components—then I'm going to end up undervaluing that electricity unit because I didn't account for the CapEx investment that I made up front. Essentially what we do is, you know, we have this number that is very flawed but that we use in the power industry that is called levelized cost of energy. What cost of energy? Levelized cost of energy. It really is a terrible metric for a variety of reasons, but it's a very simple one to think about in this context. Essentially what we do is we say how much capital did I put into building this facility and this infrastructure, and how long is it going to run and be productive? And then I look at all the OpEx over the life of that asset and I add that in also. And then I divide by the amount of units of energy that I'm going to produce. And so then, when I do that, I get a levelized cost of energy, something that helps me understand over the life of the asset how much it will cost me to actually produce each unit. And I think if I look at a GPU hour, it is a time-based metric which doesn't account for inefficiencies in electricity production, the delivery, or processing. It doesn't account for any of that. It just looks at time purely. And I think we need to move the industry to a metric where we account for those inefficiencies. Otherwise we won't value the product out of the factory appropriately and we'll end up selling it for the wrong price. Yeah, if you don't value that, that's coming out of your margins if you're not aware of it. You're eating the cost essentially. Yeah. Interesting. That's why I think if you divide effectively the cost of GPU per hour, a time-based metric, by something like the number of tokens you can produce over some time—for example, the number of tokens in an hour—all of a sudden then you can actually get the cost per token. And then you're starting to look at something that really does measure the output. It's like the cost per electron but in this case the cost per token. Another one I think is the Pareto curve, and it's looking at how many tokens can you get out of, for example, a gigawatt facility? And that way you can start to compare apples to apples in that sense and say, okay, there's only—we're in a power-constrained world, there's only so much power that is going to come into this data center. Providing we don't do something really expensive and try and change it, let's say it's static—this is a 5 megawatt data center—I want to get the most tokens out of that thing as possible. So the tokens per megawatt is the sort of ratio, the metric that I think makes a lot of sense. Yeah, it's like you almost have to have awareness of everything below the model and above it, right? If I'm going to generate code today, that's going to generate a lot of tokens. How much did that code cost me is a question that has to be walked all the way to the wasted electricity that entered the data center that was then used on the chip, which then generated the tokens all the way through, right? How He Wrote the Biggest Sci-Fi Movie of the Year

Cleo Abram(7.7M) ·59:09 ·155.3K views · 2026-04-15
Cleo Abram (Host) Andy Weir (Author, Project Hail Mary)
Science fiction author Andy Weir builds his "hard" sci-fi worlds through rigorous scientific derivation, using complex spreadsheets to ensure fictional elements—like alien biology and interstellar fuel—adhere to the laws of physics or logical "wrong math.
TL;DR Science fiction author Andy Weir builds his "hard" sci-fi worlds through rigorous scientific derivation, using complex spreadsheets to ensure fictional elements—like alien biology and interstellar fuel—adhere to the laws of physics or logical "wrong math." Scientific...

TL;DR

Science fiction author Andy Weir builds his "hard" sci-fi worlds through rigorous scientific derivation, using complex spreadsheets to ensure fictional elements—like alien biology and interstellar fuel—adhere to the laws of physics or logical "wrong math."

The Mechanics of "Hard" World-Building

  • Scientific Derivation: Weir does not arbitrarily choose plot points; he derives them from math. For *Project Hail Mary*, he calculated the exact temperature required for the fictional "Astrophage" to generate neutrinos (96.415°C) based on particle velocity and mass-energy.
  • The "Wrong Math" Spreadsheet: To maintain internal consistency, Weir created a spreadsheet specifically for "wrong math." This calculated how the alien species (the Eridians) would have over-fueled their ships because they lacked a discovery of relativity and relied on Newtonian physics.
  • Special Relativity as a Narrative Limit: The maximum distance for the Astrophage infection (eight light-years) was derived by calculating how long the organism could survive before radiating away its heat energy, adjusted for its own inertial reference frame.

Designing Rocky: Logic-Driven Alien Biology

  • Environmental Constraints: Weir started with the exoplanet 40 Eridani A b. Because the planet is close to its star (a 46-day year), he deduced it needed a thick atmosphere and a massive magnetic field to keep liquid water at 200°C without boiling away.
  • Atmospheric Physics:
      • Composition: He chose an ammonia atmosphere because ammonia is a heavy molecule that is harder for solar winds to strip away from a gravity well.
      • Planetary Shape: Due to its 6-hour rotation and proximity to the Roche limit, Weir calculated the planet would be significantly oblong, resulting in different gravity measurements at the poles versus the equator.
  • Evolutionary Outcomes:
      • Echolocation: Because no light reaches the surface through the thick atmosphere, the aliens evolved echolocation so precise it functions like vision.
      • Inorganic Construction: Eridians are described as being like a "walking beehive," where internal worker cells build a mostly inorganic carapace, resulting in pentagonal symmetry.
      • Internalized Biosphere: Since the atmosphere contains no oxygen, the aliens have an internalized system where they consume food to maintain a biological "internal biosphere" rather than exchanging gases with the environment.

Collaborative Case Study: Detecting Intelligent Life

  • Energy Signatures: When brainstorming a new story with Cleo Abram, Weir suggested looking for "energy absorption." A dead planet reflects/emits all radiation it receives; a living planet stores it in chemical bonds (plants/algae), creating a measurable deficit in radiation.
  • The "Steve’s World" Scenario: In their real-time story collaboration, they imagined an amateur astronomer detecting a sudden electromagnetic burst from a planet (not a star).
      • Technosignatures: This would prompt a search for CFCs or pollution—gases that do not occur via natural processes—indicating an industrial civilization.
  • The "Swords to Plowshares" Greeting: To signal back to a civilization 20 light-years away, Weir suggested detonating a sequence of nuclear bombs in deep space.
      • Visibility: By moving the bombs far from the Sun (e.g., near Saturn or Neptune), the contrast against the dark background of space would make the signal visible to distant telescopes.

The Role of Optimism and Public Feedback

  • Crowdsourced Fact-Checking: *The Martian* was originally published chapter-by-chapter online. Readers flagged errors, such as Weir forgetting that hydrogen and oxygen would mix evenly in the "Hab" rather than separating by weight.
  • Narrative Optimism: Weir avoids dystopias, focusing instead on "good people solving hard problems." He notes that while the news reports on misery, it is only "news" because the norm is actually humans helping one another.
  • The Purpose of Sci-Fi: For Weir, the ultimate goal is not just education but entertainment. He aims for the "that was cool" feeling that inspired him as a child.

Notable Quotes

"The reason you're seeing all this bad stuff on the news is because bad stuff is newsworthy... The kind of compassion and empathy and selflessness that humans have is so ordinary that it's not newsworthy." — Andy Weir
"I wanted my alien in my hard sci-fi book to be really alien. You can't—I wanted our environment to be completely incompatible with theirs." — Andy Weir
"The radiation [the planet] is absorbing is going into the form of life... A dead planet, like Mercury, will be emitting exactly as much radiation as it is absorbing from the sun." — Andy Weir
"I just want them to have a good time. And when they put the book away I want them to think 'that was cool.' That’s all I’m shooting for." — Andy Weir

LIVE: Karoline Leavitt Holds White House Press Briefing With Bessent And Loeffler Amid Iran Talks

Forbes Breaking News(5.5M) ·74:20 ·9.4K views · 2026-04-15
Karoline Leavitt (White House Press Secretary) Scott Bessent (Treasury Secretary) Kelly Loeffler (Administrator, Small Business Administration)
On Tax Day, the Trump administration highlighted record tax refunds and the success of "Working Families Tax Cuts" (notably no tax on tips/overtime), while confirming a maximum-pressure economic blockade on Iran and announcing the construction of a 2...
TL;DR On Tax Day, the Trump administration highlighted record tax refunds and the success of "Working Families Tax Cuts" (notably no tax on tips/overtime), while confirming a maximum-pressure economic blockade on Iran and announcing the construction of a 250-foot "Triumphal Ark"...

TL;DR

On Tax Day, the Trump administration highlighted record tax refunds and the success of "Working Families Tax Cuts" (notably no tax on tips/overtime), while confirming a maximum-pressure economic blockade on Iran and announcing the construction of a 250-foot "Triumphal Ark" for America’s 250th anniversary.

Tax Day Statistics and "Working Families" Provisions

Karoline Leavitt and Treasury Secretary Scott Bessent detailed the impact of the administration's tax policies, framing them as a "promises made, promises kept" success for the middle class.

  • Filing Data: Over 53 million filers claimed at least one signature tax cut this season.
      • The average refund for the current filing season is over $3,400.
      • More than 105 million filers used the permanently doubled standard deduction.
  • Signature Provisions:
      • No Tax on Overtime: Cited by Bessent as the most popular provision, used by 25 million filers with an average deduction of $3,100.
      • No Tax on Tips: Claimed by 6 million filers, averaging a $7,100 deduction.
      • Senior Relief: 30 million seniors claimed an enhanced deduction averaging $7,500.
      • American-Made Cars: Over 1 million filers claimed a deduction for interest on auto loans for American-made vehicles.
  • "Trump Accounts": 5 million families have opened these child savings accounts, with 1.2 million eligible for a $1,000 "seed bonus."

Small Business and Industrial Growth

SBA Administrator Kelly Loeffler reported that the administration's tax and regulatory policies have spurred a "re-industrialization" of the U.S. economy.

  • Small Business Relief: Approximately 12 million small businesses saw average tax savings of $7,000.
      • The 20% small business "pass-through" deduction has been made permanent to provide planning certainty.
  • Capital Access: The SBA set an all-time lending record of $41 billion over the past year.
  • Manufacturing Resurgence: Loeffler cited 100% expensing for equipment and R&D as the primary driver for "reshoring" industries and creating new factory shifts.
      • Mentioned the "Big Beautiful Building" at Blue Patriot Fab in Omaha as a direct result of tax incentives.

Foreign Policy and Energy Markets

The briefing addressed the ongoing economic and naval strategy regarding Iran and its impact on domestic energy costs.

  • "Operation Economic Fury": Secretary Bessent confirmed a maximum-pressure campaign, including secondary sanctions.
      • The U.S. has notified two unnamed Chinese banks that they face sanctions if they continue processing Iranian oil payments.
      • The U.S. will not renew general licenses for Russian or Iranian oil.
  • The Strait of Hormuz Blockade: The U.S. is enforcing a full blockade on vessels entering or departing Iranian ports, though freedom of navigation remains for non-Iranian traffic.
      • Mediation: Leavitt confirmed that Pakistan is the "only mediator" currently facilitating talks between the U.S. and Iran.
  • Gas Price Outlook: Bessent expressed optimism that gas prices will feature a "3" in front of them (under $4.00) between June 20 and September 20, contingent on the reopening of the Straits.

Cabinet Updates and National Landmarks

  • Federal Reserve: Secretary Bessent confirmed that Kevin Warsh's confirmation hearing for Fed Chair is scheduled for the 21st of this month.
  • Acting Attorney General: Leavitt stated that Todd Blanch remains the Acting AG; no permanent personnel updates were provided despite media speculation regarding other candidates.
  • United States Triumphal Ark: The administration will submit plans tomorrow for a 250-foot tall monument on Columbia Island in the Potomac River.
      • The structure is intended to commemorate the 250th anniversary of American independence (Semiquincentennial).
"If the Democrats in Congress had gotten their way and blocked President Trump’s historic legislation, Americans would have faced the largest tax hike in history—nearly $4 trillion. The average taxpayer would have seen a 22% increase in their tax rates."

— Karoline Leavitt

"Iran is—or used to be—the largest state sponsor of terrorism... we have told countries that if you are buying Iranian oil, if Iranian money is sitting in your banks, we are now willing to apply secondary sanctions."

— Scott Bessent

"Not a single worker, not a single small business asked to pay a dollar more in taxes. Yet, every single congressional Democrat voted to raise taxes... Thankfully, Republicans united behind the common sense policy of letting Americans keep more of what they earn."

— Kelly Loeffler

OMB Dir. Russell Vought testifies before House panel on Trump's FY 2027 budget request—4/15/26

CNBC Television(3.4M) ·187:47 ·4.7K views · 2026-04-15
Russell Vought (Director, Office of Management and Budget)
OMB Director Russell Vought presented a FY 2027 budget proposal centered on a historic 42% increase in defense spending ($1.5 trillion) and deep non-defense cuts, while defending the administration’s controversial use of "impoundments" to withhold congressionally appropriated funds.
TL;DR OMB Director Russell Vought presented a FY 2027 budget proposal centered on a historic 42% increase in defense spending ($1.5 trillion) and deep non-defense cuts, while defending the administration’s controversial use of "impoundments" to withhold congressionally...

TL;DR

OMB Director Russell Vought presented a FY 2027 budget proposal centered on a historic 42% increase in defense spending ($1.5 trillion) and deep non-defense cuts, while defending the administration’s controversial use of "impoundments" to withhold congressionally appropriated funds.

FY 2027 Budget Priorities & Fiscal Strategy

  • Defense vs. Non-Defense: The budget requests $1.5 trillion for defense—a "paradigm-shifting" 42% increase—balanced by a 10% cut to non-defense discretionary spending.
  • Agency Eliminations: Vought confirmed the administration's intent to fully shutter the U.S. Agency for International Development (USAID) and the Corporation for Public Broadcasting (NPR/PBS), citing "mismanagement" and "woke programming."
  • Law Enforcement Surge: The proposal includes a 13% increase for the Department of Justice, specifically to combat violent crime and establish a new "National Fraud Division."
  • Economic Assumptions: The administration claims a 10% reduction in the deficit due to growth and the "Big Beautiful Bill," though the Minority cited CBO estimates suggesting the bill adds $4.7 trillion to the long-term debt.

The Impoundment & Executive Power Conflict

  • Defending Impoundments: Vought explicitly stated, "Of course I believe in impoundments," arguing that the President has the constitutional right to spend less than Congress provides.
  • Disregarding Oversight: When questioned on GAO findings that the administration illegally withheld billions for NIH and Head Start, Vought dismissed the GAO as "typically wrong" and "partisan."
  • "Fiscal Futility": Vought characterized the administration's aggressive withholding of funds as an end to "decades of fiscal futility," asserting that the executive branch must use every "fiscal toolbox" to curb spending.

Social Safety Net & Healthcare Reforms

  • Medicaid & Work Requirements: The budget leans on "historic welfare reform" by implementing work requirements for able-bodied adults.
  • Healthcare Coverage: Ranking Member Brendan Boyle (D-PA) highlighted CBO data suggesting 15 million Americans will lose healthcare coverage; Vought countered that these individuals were "ineligible, illegal, or able-bodied adults refusing to work."
  • Elimination of Assistance Programs: The administration proposes zeroing out the Low-Income Home Energy Assistance Program (LIHEAP) and the Capital Magnet Fund, labeling them "historically fraudulent" or "woke."

Energy, Inflation, and Geopolitics

  • The "War of Choice": Minority members criticized the $350 billion set aside for a "war of choice" with Iran, arguing it has spiked gas prices (exceeding $4.00/gallon) and drained resources from domestic programs like childcare.
  • Tariff Revenue: Vought projected that "America First" tariffs would generate approximately $4.7 trillion in revenue, serving as a primary pillar of the administration's fiscal stabilization plan.
  • Foreign Aid Cuts: The budget terminates various international programs, including funds for green energy in Vietnam and "gender-affirming care" in Guatemala, refocusing those dollars on the "Department of War."

Notable Quotes

"Fiscal futility has ended with the return of President Trump. In just one year, the Trump administration and Congress have made historic progress on righting our fiscal ship." — Russell Vought, OMB Director
"A budget is a statement of our priorities... This budget sees the hungry, it sees the homeless, it sees the vulnerable, and it turns away." — Rep. Morgan McGarvey (D-KY)
"The Supreme Court has never spoken on this issue [impoundment]... We believe we have the moral and legal high ground every time we make a decision." — Russell Vought, OMB Director
"We are paying people not to work... Trump comes in, and in one year, secures the border, stops the flow, and pulls down the drive of our deficit." — Rep. Jodey Arrington (R-TX), Chairman

Upon signing this year's appropriations bills, President Trump continued to bring discretionary spending under control. The full 2026 appropriations bills enacted the first real spending cut in over a decade to discretionary spending. It rooted out wasteful spending that the administration identified across federal agencies. The enacted bills reaffirm administration policies to eliminate ineffective federal agencies that do not serve a useful purpose. For example, the bills eliminated all programming for USAID as the administration works to fully shut down the agency after decades of mismanagement and abuse. This also included the complete elimination of the Corporation for Public Broadcasting, which funneled billions of taxpayer dollars into biased and woke programming at NPR and PBS. We look forward to working with you to achieve even more progress in the next appropriation cycle. This budget builds on the president's vision by continuing to constrain non-defense spending and reform the federal government. The budget proposes a 10% cut to non-defense compared to 2026 levels. Within this total, the budget maintains investment in border security and immigration enforcement. It delivers on the president's commitment to support law enforcement and combat violent crime with a 13% increase to the Department of Justice and honors the nation's sacred obligation to our military veterans. In addition, this 2027 budget eliminates wasteful Green New Deal projects that are dependent on foreign supply chains, returns control of education back to American families, and roots out waste, fraud, and abuse in programs both at home and abroad, ensuring that every taxpayer dollar makes Americans safer, stronger, and more prosperous. The budget simultaneously builds upon the historic $1 trillion fiscal year 26 defense topline by requesting 1.5 trillion for 2027, a 42% increase as promised by President Trump last year. Don't get me wrong, executive authority exists, but it does not extend to the administration unilaterally withholding funding that's been appropriated by Congress. The law mandates it. Impoundments occur when the administration refuses to implement federal expenditures authorized and appropriated by Congress and enacted in law. In fact, there have been 235 years of constitutional interpretation, case law, and statute which have made it clear that once a law is enacted, the executive branch is not permitted to refuse to execute that law. An impoundment would basically replace the rule of law with the rule of one person. Article 1, Section 9 of the Constitution clearly gives Congress the power of the purse; it states that no money shall be drawn from the Treasury but in consequence of appropriations made by law. The Constitution mandates that the President take care that the laws be faithfully executed. As we heard today, in 1973, President Nixon attempted to impound billions of dollars of enacted clean water appropriations over the objection of Congress. As you know, in *Train v. City of New York*, the Supreme Court unanimously disagreed with President Nixon. That decision was even relied upon as precedent in the court's '98 decision striking down President Clinton's use of a line-item veto to block certain portions of a spending bill. Nixon's attempt at impoundment was the basis for the '74 Impoundment Control Act, which banned impoundments. Despite that law and those Supreme Court rulings, this President and this administration believe that impoundment is within the President's power. Your General Counsel argued that nearly all enacted laws are merely just suggestions and guidelines that give the President and the executive branch substantial discretion to follow, alter, or even disregard.
