TL;DR

The global economy faces a "shock upon shock" scenario driven by conflict in the Middle East, with growth projections slowing to 3.1% (and potentially 2% in adverse cases) as public debt is on track to reach post-WWII levels by 2029. Managing Director Kristalina Georgieva urges member nations to prioritize macroeconomic stability, avoid unargeted subsidies, and implement structural reforms to build resilience against a 21st-century landscape of frequent disruption.

Global Economic Outlook and Risks

  • Revised Growth Projections: Global growth is slowing from 3.4% last year to a projected 3.1% in 2026.
      • Adverse Scenario: If the Middle East conflict persists and oil prices remain high, growth could drop to 2%.
  • Inflationary Pressures: While long-term inflation expectations remain anchored, short-term projections for 2026 have been upgraded due to supply chain disruptions.
      • Commodity Spillovers: Significant concern regarding fertilizer prices (which have doubled in some regions), which threatens global food security.
  • Supply Chain Disruptions: Georgieva described the current crisis as a "20th-century type of shock in the 21st century."
      • Logistics Lag: Tankers are slow-moving vessels; a disruption in the Strait of Hormuz takes roughly 40 days to impact distant markets like Fiji, meaning the full effect of recent events has yet to be felt.
      • Energy Intensity: Shortages are appearing in Asia for oil, gas, naphtha, and helium.

Strategic Policy Guidance

  • Monetary Policy: Georgieva advises a "look before you leap" approach.
      • Wait and See: Countries with high credibility and anchored expectations should pause before raising rates further.
      • Early Action: Central banks lacking credibility may need to send stronger signals to protect price stability.
  • Fiscal Discipline: Global public debt is projected to breach 100% of GDP by 2029.
      • Sustainability vs. Protection: Policymakers must balance fiscal health with protecting the most vulnerable.
      • Warning Against Unargeted Measures: The IMF strongly discourages broad-based tax cuts, export controls, and energy subsidies, noting they "prolong the pain" of high prices.
  • Structural Reform: Governments are urged to lift productivity through reforms now rather than waiting for shocks to pass. "A strong economy is the best buffer."

Regional Impacts and Vulnerabilities

  • MENA Region: Facing the most severe impact, with growth projections downgraded by 2.3% to a total of 1.4% for 2026. Five out of eight regional oil producers are expected to see negative growth.
  • Sub-Saharan Africa: Highly vulnerable due to import dependency and limited fiscal space. The IMF is seeing a surge in demand for programs in this region to address fuel shortages and debt.
  • Asia-Pacific: While India remains a "bright spot" with growth twice the global average, the broader region is struggling with energy import dependency.
      • China: Projections were slightly downgraded (4.5% to 4.4%), but the IMF sees potential if China successfully shifts its model toward domestic consumption.
  • Climate Vulnerability: Small island states like Jamaica are facing "repetitive" shocks. The IMF advocates for regional insurance pooling and building "climate-sensitive" infrastructure.

The IMF’s "Firefighter" Role

  • Financial Support: The IMF anticipates near-term demand for financial support to range between $20 billion and $50 billion.
  • Active Programs: Currently managing 39 programs, with at least a dozen new requests pending, primarily from African nations.
  • Toolbox Adaptation: The IMF is conducting comprehensive reviews of program design, conditionality, and surveillance to sharpen policy advice.
  • Debt Resolution: Ongoing work through the Global Sovereign Debt Roundtable aims to address the dangerously high levels of public debt globally.

***

Notable Quotes

"What is different this time in comparison to COVID is the cumulative impact of shock upon shock. It has pushed debt to dangerously high levels. Global public debt is on track to breach 100% of GDP in 2029, a level not seen since the aftermath of World War II." — Kristalina Georgieva
"My advice: look before you leap on monetary policy. For countries where monetary policy was well calibrated before the shock and expectations remain anchored, wait and see is the right approach." — Kristalina Georgieva
"In fact, what we have is a 20th-century type of shock—slower-moving shock—in the 21st century... A tanker is a slow-moving vessel. It would take 40 days to get all the way to Fiji." — Kristalina Georgieva
"The most important thing we do for our members is to help them help themselves—to have strong policies during good times to build buffers so during bad times they can protect their people." — Kristalina Georgieva
The global policy agenda. As usual, I will first turn to Managing Director Kristalina Georgieva for some opening remarks, and then we will open for your questions. Kristalina, the floor is yours. Thank you very much, Julie. Good morning to all those who are here in the room and those who are online. Like all of you, we have been watching closely the events in the Middle East—a war that causes significant pain on people and the economies in the region and around the world. My hopes and my prayers are for the current ceasefire to lead to a durable peace. The impact on the global economy is already large. Even if the conflict is short-lived, extensive infrastructure damage and supply chain disruptions are pushing prices up and slowing global growth down from 3.4% last year to 3.1% in 2026. But if the conflict persists and oil prices stay high for an extended period, we must brace for tough times ahead. Our World Economic Outlook outlines a range of scenarios. In the most adverse case, growth could fall to 2%. And the shock is global; all countries are affected by higher energy prices. But the negative impact is highly asymmetric, with the biggest burdens falling on countries that import energy and have limited policy space. In many cases, these are low-income or fragile economies. They need attention, and an important focus of our discussion this week is on how we can best support them in this world of frequent shocks and uncertainty. What is our policy advice to our members in the short term? Maintaining macroeconomic and financial stability is key. Understandably, governments would like to help businesses and people hit by the exogenous supply shock. My advice: look before you leap on monetary policy. For countries where monetary policy was well-calibrated before the shock and expectations remain anchored, "wait and see" is the right approach. In other countries, early policy action may be required. On fiscal policy, we have been warning for some time that public debt is constraining fiscal space. I want to stress that what is different this time, in comparison to COVID, is the cumulative impact of shock upon shock. It has pushed debt to dangerously high levels. Global public debt is on track to breach 100% of GDP in 2029, a level not seen since the aftermath of World War II. To maintain their fiscal policy credibility, policymakers need to strike a careful balance between safeguarding fiscal sustainability and protecting those who are hit the hardest and have the least capacity to respond. The good news is that many countries have so far avoided untargeted tax cuts, energy subsidies, and price controls. The not-so-good news is that we are seeing some countries putting in place untargeted measures, export controls, or broad-based tax cuts. While the intention behind these measures may be good—it is to protect people from the shock—such untargeted actions will only prolong the pain of high prices. Finally, even as policymakers respond to the short-term effects of this shock, they must not lose sight of broader forces affecting the global economy, from geopolitics and trade to technology, demographics, and climate. We must adapt our policy to these long-term trends by accelerating growth-oriented reforms because these reforms will protect us from the shocks to come. Policymakers will need to carry out structural reforms to lift productivity and growth. A strong economy is the best buffer. And once this shock passes, then we need to rebuild policy space. Now, let me turn to the role of the IMF. As our global policy agenda makes clear, we serve as the firefighter for our member countries, and we are committed to helping them navigate this complex landscape. We anticipate near-term demand for IMF financial support to range between $20 and $50 billion. This represents augmentations of some existing programs. Currently, we have 39 programs and prospective demand for new programs from at least a dozen countries, a number of them in Sub-Saharan Africa. We are coordinating closely with the World Bank, with the International Energy Agency, and with other partners, including at the regional level, to maximize our combined response. Even as we step up our support, we continue to adapt our toolkit. We are doing very important reviews—review of program design and conditionality, comprehensive surveillance review, and review of our FSAP—so we can further sharpen the policy advice we provide to our members. We continue to work on countries on that, including through the Global Sovereign Debt Roundtable. It will meet again this afternoon. And as our member countries navigate this moment of uncertainty, we are helping them think ahead so they can handle any form of turbulence. We love our membership. Thank you. Thank you very much, Kristalina. We'll now open the floor for questions. When called on, we will bring you a microphone. Please give your name and your affiliation. We'll start over on this side, gentleman in the gray jacket right here. Sure. Thanks, Faisal Islam, BBC News. Thanks, Managing Director. Clearly, there's been a market price impact from the events in the Strait of Hormuz. To what extent are you worried about any physical supply chain breakages as a result of what's sort of locked in to the Gulf? And just to clarify the advice to central banks on this wave of inflation: are you saying just sort of pause? Don't think you have to chase this inflation rate first and foremost, and don't think you have to raise interest rates kind of almost preemptively. Thank you for your question. Yes, we are concerned about the physical breakdown in supply chains. We are already seeing, especially in Asia, which is highly dependent on imports from the Gulf, that there are shortages in place not only of oil and gas but also of naphtha and of helium. That is creating already some disruptions, and we need to recognize it is not going to evaporate overnight even if the war ends tomorrow. Why? Because a tanker is a slow-moving vessel. It would take 40 days to get all the way to Fiji. So we need to be prepared that the impact of the supply disruptions in the weeks ahead is going to be to deepen. So what do we tell countries? Use measures that can economize energy. I have seen some countries doing exactly that: putting in place incentives like making public transport free, incentivizing people to take it, or working from home. We did it during COVID. I don't see a reason why not to do more of it now or switch to less energy-intensive activities over time. So that is where we are. I'll give you just one example. Jet fuel: something that I learned yesterday is that Korea is a big producer of jet fuel. They are an oil importer but a jet fuel exporter. Fortunately for the world, they're one of the countries in Asia that has strong reserves of oil and gas and that allows the economy to function well. But they need new supplies and, as I said, the tankers are slow to move. In fact, what we have is a 20th-century type of shock—a slower-moving shock in the 21st century. To your second question on advice to central banks: what we tell central banks is, if you have high credibility, signal that your objective is to protect price stability, but don't rush. Wait to see how conditions would evolve. If we are to move faster out of the war, it may not be necessary to take action. But for central banks that do not have that credibility, they may need to utilize stronger signals. So it is going to be country-specific. Okay, let's go to the middle. Gentleman sort of in toward the back of the middle. Yeah, you. Thanks very much. Miles McCormack with the Financial Times. Managing Director, I wanted to ask you a bit on the inflationary outlook. Obviously, we've already seen signs of inflationary pass-through in agriculture, logistics, retail, etc. How concerned are you in the near term about the pervasiveness of the inflationary shock and its stickiness even after the war comes to an end? We have upgraded our projections for inflation for 2026. We were on a very good trajectory of inflation going down. Now there is somewhat of a reversal, and what we see is that short-term inflation expectations have moved up here in the United States. They have moved up in the Euro area. The good news for now is that long-term expectations remain anchored, and that signals that we are still at the time when a faster resolution of hostilities is possible. When we look at the risks of the impact of inflation through interruptions of supply chains—the question that the BBC asked—yes, we are concerned about risks for inflation moving into food prices. Should the delivery of fertilizers at a reasonable price not be restarted soon, then in this case we might see pressure on food prices. We all recognize how difficult this would be for people everywhere. We were looking at, for example, urea sales in Africa; already from $400, it jumped to $800. The price has doubled. These are dangerous developments, and this is why we are so much urging a resolution that would reduce the risk of inflation spillovers. Okay, we'll go right here. Woman in the second row in the dark blue right at the end. Yep. Good morning, Rosario with Bloomberg. For now, the talk of peace means that even less oil is flowing from the Strait of Hormuz. We haven't really seen, as you say, the supply chain effects, and shipments are arriving at the ports as you've mentioned. Do you see that the effects will spread first slowly and then very quickly if the conflict extends? Will this hit economic growth even more? And if you can also say, do you think that there is a mispricing from markets on the conflict? Thank you so much. We look at the impact of interruptions of the supply of oil and gas with great concern because it has significance across the board in economies. We recognize that how big the impact would be on inflation and on growth would depend on the longevity of this interruption of supplies and on the scale of damage to energy infrastructure in the Gulf. What we also recognize is that March was a tough month, but April is likely to be even tougher. Why? Because the tankers that left by February 28 have reached their destinations and there are no new deliveries coming. This is why we are urging countries to take measures to reduce energy intensity. Take them now. Don't wait for weeks from now. We see the shock energizing that kind of thinking about appropriate measures to reduce demand, and I want to remind us all that in previous shocks this is what has happened. It wasn't that long ago when Europe was freeing itself from dependency on Russian oil and gas. What I want to remind people is that Europe did it successfully, and it did it with a very significant energy efficiency leap as part of this adjustment. We also know that there would be new sources of energy coming. There would be new routes in place. The problem is that the latter—these new routes and new sources—would take time, a year or a year and a half. In the meanwhile, what countries have is good policies that would reduce the size of this equilibrium. Again, I think it is a very good time to apply those good policies everywhere. All right, let's go to this side of the room. I'm going to take the woman at the very end in the black blazer. Thank you. I'm Bridget Uguay from African News. We've heard this week that Sub-Saharan African economies are likely to be the most affected by the war in the Middle East. We're already seeing the effects with fuel shortages spreading across the region. And you just mentioned how the war has been driving demands for new IMF programs, including demands from Sub-Saharan African countries. My question is: how concretely can you help them? Let me first say that we have been very loud on the asymmetric impact of this war. I have in my office a map of countries and where they fall in terms of their dependency on imports and their fiscal space, and it pains me that the majority of Sub-Saharan African countries are in this quadrant of vulnerability. Therefore, we are very determined to use this week to identify which are the countries that most urgently need support and then come out of the week with discussions around the way we would support them. But let me emphasize that the most important thing we do for our members is to help them help themselves: to have strong policies during good times to build buffers so during bad times they can protect their people. I am impressed by how much countries have done, including countries in Sub-Saharan Africa. I was with the African Consultative Group yesterday, and I can tell you the ministers and the central bank governors didn't ask for money. They asked for urgent policy advice. They asked for our support to develop further the local currency market in Africa. But of course there would be need for financial support and, as I said, we are prepared and we would move very swiftly to respond to requests from countries. Actually, my message to the membership is: if you need help financially, don't hesitate. Move fast, because the sooner we act, the more we would protect the economy and people. All right. I'm going to stay on this side of the room. I'm going to go to the woman in the front row with the tan blazer. Yes, please go ahead. Hello, Fatma Ibrahim from Al-Ahram magazine. My question is: have any countries in the MENA region, like Egypt and similarly affected ones, formally approached the IMF for more financing support? And what are the updates regarding this request? Also, what has the IMF assessed for the measures taken by oil-importing countries? It is very rewarding to see how Egypt, having implemented difficult reforms, is now in a better position to face this shock. In fact, we are not at this point discussing augmentation of the Egypt program because the country—yes, it is affected. We have seen that both in terms of some currency depreciation and in terms of the impact on energy prices domestically, but the government has acted very responsibly. This is a case when the measures put in place are the right measures. They are well-targeted. We use the fact that we have built a well-targeted social protection system in Egypt over the last years, and they are very well-calibrated within the fiscal space. Egypt has, of course—should conditions worsen—a program that we can review from a perspective of "should we do some more." But at this point we see Egypt as one of the good examples of a country that has gone through difficult reforms, has built responsible policies, and is acting in a way that helps the people that most need this help. When we look at the broader Middle East region, I can say with great sorrow that the impact on growth there is the most severe. We are actually downgrading growth projections for MENA the most, by 2.3%. When you look at the distribution of this downgrade, not surprisingly, the oil and gas exporters see the biggest shrinkage of their economies. So we go down to 1.4% for 2026 for MENA. Five out of the eight countries that are oil producers see their growth go into negative territory because of this shock. Okay, very good. Let's go. Thank you very much. Amitabh Sharma. I'm from the Gleaner from Jamaica. Managing Director, I would like to also... I mean, we are looking at the Middle Eastern war which is causing a lot of problems, but also I would like your insights on climate change, which is a huge problem, especially after Hurricane Melissa where half the western side of the island got flattened out. That's a recurring problem. The storms are becoming more severe and the weather patterns are becoming more severe, too. How does Jamaica specifically—or the Caribbean—have those buffers in place to move forward? We cannot control the storms, but how can we protect ourselves? Thank you. It is such a painful story, the one of Jamaica, because of the hit of the hurricane. Actually, you had two hurricanes following each other. We see that happening in many climate-vulnerable countries on a repetitive basis, more frequently and with more severe impact. We have across the world this climate phenomenon becoming more of a threat to countries. In the case of Jamaica, we actually did provide emergency financing. We were part of a coordinated coordination group with the World Bank, the Inter-American Development Bank, and of course under the leadership of the Jamaican government. The Prime Minister personally took it upon himself to make sure that international support is well-coordinated and as impactful as possible. So what can Jamaica do? What can countries do? First, it is important to recognize we're not going to go back; we would go forward in a more climate-sensitive place for the economies. Like Jamaica, it is crucial to build resilience to these shocks both in terms of how infrastructure is constructed but also in terms of fiscal buffers and insurance products. Jamaica is one of the leaders of using insurance, and it helped tremendously. I believe that the Caribbean, which has been doing quite a lot, should work even more closely together. This is one piece of good news: integration. Small countries cannot easily access insurance products alone, but when they pull together, they can do it. Learning from each other, taking those precautionary measures together, but also finding opportunities to strengthen growth and work in a way that enhances the viability of their economies—this is a very positive development, and we have been supporting it and we will continue to support them. And just to say, you know, for all of us here in this room: it's tough when you are in a big country. Imagine what it is to be on an island. Imagine what it is if you are on an island in the Caribbean or in the Pacific and you're on the end of a very long chain. The Pacific is even in a tougher place, on a very long supply chain. So as we think of our difficulties, spare a thought for those most at risk. Let's go over here. Woman at the very end here. Yes, thank you. Hello, I'm Katherine Chan from BusinessWorld in the Philippines. So, Managing Director, you've touched on the looming oil shortages in Asia, but I wanted to just zoom in a little bit. How is this conflict affecting ASEAN economies? And of course, given these shocks, what policy steps now should ASEAN governments prioritize to weather these shocks and boost their resilience as well as possibly attract investments amid this? Thank you. ASEAN countries have been very determined to put in place good policies and strong institutions. They have done it over the last years. Actually, ASEAN is a bright spot in terms of growth and in terms of economic dynamism. When we look at the impact of this shock, because of this strong buildup over the years, ASEAN is actually weathering the shock as a group of countries relatively well. But there is a big difference across ASEAN countries. Most ASEAN countries are energy importers. There are some that are exporters; they have that easier position. For the energy importers, the situation is much more difficult, and I very much sympathize with the people in the Philippines because I know that your country does face that difficulty. ASEAN has chosen a path to regional integration and more regional trade and more cooperation on the big transformative trends in the world: on technology, on AI, on trade. They have taken very important steps to strengthen their interconnectivity, and the Philippines is now leading the ASEAN. I am going to be there when the meeting takes place. And I do believe that this is very important for regions that have the potential to trade more within the countries of the region. Build that integration; you will benefit from it in a more shock-prone world. All right, let's go right here to the middle. The second row, woman in the black blazer. In Aadinska, Ukraine textur. How could the current war in the Gulf affect another major war in the continent, the Russia-Ukrainian war, in both economic and security terms, given that the aggressor, Russia, may stand to gain high oil and gas revenues? Thank you. The people of Ukraine have gone through a very difficult time, and it's not yet over. Five years in a war. And of course, this being a negative energy supply shock—given that the energy sector has been impacted quite severely where Russia bombarded big parts of the energy infrastructure—that makes the situation in Ukraine even more difficult. Wars are bad news for people. They're bad news for the economy. And I think it would be the aspiration of all of us in this room: there is no better way to help the world to help its own people but for the wars to end. I think that the negative impact from the Gulf War overall—even for countries that benefit from being exporters and benefit from higher prices—the negative impact is there. Nobody is spared. I have a question for the audience. How many of you really want all these wars to end? Raise a hand. I want to see where we are. Okay. We are done. No, we have time for just maybe a few more questions. So let me just go to the very end here. The woman in the black blazer, first row. Thank you very much. Karishma here from Business Today, India Today Group. Managing Director, you spoke about the ASEAN economy. I want to just take a broader regional look for Asia-Pacific, especially the fact that the economic outlook report did raise the fact that the regional outlook is weakening, but with high hopes on countries like China and India. I know you've been always optimistic about India, saying that it's a resilient economy. If the situation gets severe, do you think emerging economies like India and also advanced economies like China will be able to—will have the appetite to—bear the brunt? The most impressive development over the last decades has been the advancements in policies and institutions in emerging market economies. We did a review of monetary and fiscal policy in emerging market economies. In monetary policy, many of them are as good—of course there are exceptions—but the majority are as good or better than advanced economies. In fiscal policy, there is still some space for further improvements, but countries are determined to move in that way. So we have a more resilient world because of the resilience of many emerging market economies that have put in place independent central banks and fiscal councils and built very strong reserves. They benefit from it. But there is also a positive spillover for everybody else. In an adverse scenario, of course, everybody would face a more difficult time. And if the impact of hostilities is to be prolonged, then we are going to see a slowdown in global growth. As I said in my opening, it could go all the way to 2%. And yet we would see economies that have these strong fundamentals have dynamism. Like India does: growth is more than two times higher than the average global growth, and that comes because of this strength of fundamentals. So we do not see a scenario under which there would be a dramatic development. Of course, there is one issue that we all need to be very watchful of, and it is financial stability. That for now seems to be holding relatively well. Let it be that way in the future. And I can add to this: Asia is very severely impacted because of that dependency of imports. But we see Asia once again demonstrating that good performance historically is helping. And that story of relentless pursuit of good policies and building strong institutions is what we see in Asia. All right, I'm going to take one final question. I'm going to go right here, front row, the woman in the mint green. Thank you. I'm Toshi with China Central Television. My question is about China. So with the global growth becoming more uncertain, can China help keep the world economy stable, and where do you see the biggest opportunity for international cooperation? Well, it is a continuation of the answer I gave to the previous question. What we see in China—while we are slightly downgrading projections for growth from 4.5 to 4.4%—is that we do see resilience in the economy. China has great potential as it moves to change its growth model from one that is mostly dependent on exports to one that is primarily oriented towards domestic consumption. Move from goods to services; that can give a big boost to China. And of course China's size means that when China does well, there are positive spillovers for the rest of the world, for the region especially for Asia, but also for the rest of the world. All right, thank you very much everyone, and thank you, Managing Director. This concludes our press briefing. The full transcript of the press briefing will be made available on our website at Media Relations, and we wish you all a wonderful day today. Thank you.

Are you saying just sort of pause, don't think you have to chase this inflation rate first and foremost, and don't think you have to raise interest rates kind of almost preemptively? Thank you for your question. Yes, we are concerned about the physical breakdown in supply chains. We are already seeing, especially in Asia that is highly dependent on imports from the Gulf, that there are shortages in place, not only of oil and gas, but also of naphtha and of helium, and that is creating already some disruptions. And that, we need to recognize, is not going to evaporate overnight even if the war ends tomorrow. Why? Because a tanker is a slow-moving vessel. It would take 40 days to get all the way to Fiji. So we need to be prepared that the impact of these supply disruptions in the weeks ahead is going to deepen. So what do we tell countries? Use measures that can economize energy. And I have seen some countries doing exactly that—putting in place incentives like making public transport free, incentivizing people to take it, or work from home. We did it during COVID; I don't see a reason why not to do more of it now. Or switch to a less energy-intensive activities over time. So that is where we are. I'll give you just one example: jet fuel. Something that I learned yesterday is that Korea is a big producer of jet fuel. They are an oil importer but a jet fuel exporter. And fortunately for the world, they're one of the countries in Asia that has strong reserves of oil and gas, and that allows the economy to function well. But they need new supplies, and as I said, the tankers are slow to move. In fact, what we have is a 20th-century type of shock—a slower-moving shock—in the 21st century. To your second question on advice to central banks. What we tell central banks is if you have high credibility, signal that your objective is to protect price stability, but don't rush. Wait to see how conditions would evolve. If we are to move faster out of the war, it may not be necessary to take action. But for central banks that do not have that credibility, they may need to utilize stronger signals. So it is going to be country-specific. Okay. Let's go to the middle. Gentleman toward the back of the middle. Yep. You. Thanks very much. Myles McCormick with the Financial Times. Managing Director, I wanted to ask you a bit on the inflationary outlook. Obviously, we've already seen signs of inflationary pass-through in agriculture, logistics, retail, etc. How concerned are you in the near term about the pervasiveness of the inflationary shock and its stickiness, even after the war comes to an end? We have upgraded our projections for inflation for 2026. We were on a very good trajectory of inflation going down; now there is somewhat of a reversal. And what we see is that short-term inflation expectations have moved up. Here in the United States, they have moved up, and in the euro area. But the good news for now is that long-term inflation expectations remain well-anchored. And that signals that we are still at a time when a faster resolution of hostilities is possible. When we look at the risks of the impact of inflation through interruptions of supply chain—the question that BBC asked—yes, we are concerned about risks for inflation moving into food prices. If the delivery of fertilizers at a reasonable price is not restarted soon, then in this case, we might see pressure on food prices. And we all recognize how difficult this would be for people everywhere. We were looking at, for example, urea sales in Africa. Already from 400 dollars it jumped to 800 dollars. It doubled. Price has doubled. These are dangerous developments, and this is why we are so much urging a resolution that would reduce the risk of inflation spillovers. Okay. We'll go right here. Woman in the second row in the dark blue, right at the end. Yep. Good morning. Jorgelina do Rosario with Bloomberg. For now, the talk of peace means that even less oil is flowing from the Straits of Hormuz. We haven't really seen, as you say, the supply chain effects, and shipments are arriving at the ports, as you've mentioned. Do you see that the effects will spread first slowly and then very quickly if the conflict stands? And this will hit even more economic growth. And if you can also say, do you think that there is a mispricing from markets on the conflict? Thank you so much. We look at the impact of interruptions of supply of oil and gas with great concern because it has significance across the board in economies. And we recognize that how big the impact would be on growth—on inflation and on growth—would depend on the longevity of this interruption of supplies and on the scale of damage on energy infrastructure in the Gulf. What we also recognize is that March was a tough month, but April is likely to be even tougher. Why? Because the tankers that left by February 28 have reached their destinations and there are no new deliveries coming. And this is why we are urging countries to take measures to reduce energy intensity—take them now, don't wait for weeks from now. We see the shock energizing that kind of thinking about appropriate measures to reduce demand. And I want to remind us all that in previous shocks, this is what has happened. It wasn't that long ago when Europe was freeing itself from dependency on Russian oil and gas. And what I want to remind people is Europe did it successfully, and it did it with a very significant energy efficiency leap as part of this adjustment. We also know that there would be new sources of energy coming; there would be new routes in place. The problem is that the latter—these new routes, new sources—it would take time, a year, a year and a half. In meanwhile, what countries have is good policies that would reduce the size of the disequilibrium. And I, again, I think it is a very good time to apply those good policies everywhere. All right. Let's go to this side of the room. I'm going to take the woman at the very end in the black blazer. Thank you. I'm Bridget Ugwe from Africanews. We've heard this week that sub-Saharan African economies are likely to be the most affected by the war in the Middle East. We're already seeing the effect with fuel shortages spreading across the region. And you just mentioned how the war has been driving demand for new IMF programs, including demand from sub-Saharan African countries. My question is, how concretely can you help them? Thank you. Let me first say that we have been very loud on the asymmetric impact of this war. And I have in my office a map of countries and where they fall in terms of their dependency on imports and their fiscal space. And it pains me that the majority of sub-Saharan Africa countries are in this quadrant of vulnerability. Therefore, we are very determined to use this week to identify which are the countries that most urgently need support and then come out of the week with discussions around the way we would support them. But let me emphasize that the most important thing we do for our members is to help them help themselves—to have strong policies during good times to build buffers, so during bad times they can protect their people. And I am impressed by how much countries have done, including countries in sub-Saharan Africa. I was with the African Consultative Group yesterday, and I can tell you the ministers, the central bank governors, didn't ask for money. They asked for urgent policy advice. They asked for our support to develop further the local currency markets in Africa. But of course, there would be need for financial support, and as I said, we are prepared and we would move very swiftly to respond to requests from countries. And actually, my message to the membership is if you need help financially, don't hesitate, move fast. Because the sooner we act, the more we would protect the economy and people. All right. I'm going to stay on this side of the room. I'm going to go to the woman in the front row with the kind of tan blazer. Yes. Please go ahead. Hello. Fatma Ibrahim from Amwal Al Ghad Magazine. My question is, has any country in the MENA region, like Egypt and similarly affected ones, formally approached the IMF for more financing support? And what are the updates regarding this request? Also, what has the IMF assessed regarding the measures taken by oil-importing countries to reduce the impact of the war? Thank you. It is very rewarding to see how Egypt, having implemented difficult reforms, is now in a better position to face this shock. And in fact, we are not at this point discussing augmentation of the Egypt program because the country, yes, it is affected—we have seen that both in terms of some currency depreciation and in terms of the impact on energy prices domestically—but the government has acted very responsibly. This is a case where measures put in place are the right measures. They are well-targeted. We use the fact that we have built a well-targeted social protection system in Egypt over the last years, and they are very well-calibrated within the fiscal space Egypt has. Of course, should conditions worsen, we have a program that we of course can review from a perspective of should we do some more. But at this point, we see Egypt as one of the good examples of a country that has gone through difficult reforms, has built responsible policies, and is acting in a way that helps people that most need this help. When we look at the broader Middle East region, I can say with great sorrow that the impact on growth there is the most severe. We are actually downgrading growth projections for MENA the most, by 2.3%. Down by 2.3%, which is—what did it bring MENA's growth? And when you look at the distribution of this downgrade, not surprisingly, the oil exporters, oil and gas exporters, see the biggest shrinkage of their economies. Actually, we go down to 1.4% for 2026 for MENA. Five out of the eight countries that are oil producers, their growth goes into negative territory because of this shock. Very good. Let's go back to the middle of the room. I'm going to go to the gentleman third row on the end here. Thank you very much. Amitabh Sharma, I'm from The Gleaner from Jamaica. Managing Director, I would like your insights on climate change, which is a huge problem, especially after Hurricane Melissa where the western side of the island got flattened out. That's a recurring problem. The storms are becoming more severe and the weather patterns are becoming more severe too. How do—well, Jamaica specifically, the Caribbean—how do we have those buffers in place and move forward? We cannot control the storms, but how can we protect ourselves? Thank you. It is such a painful story, the one of Jamaica, because of the hit of the hurricane. Actually, you had two hurricanes following each other. We see that happening in many climate-vulnerable countries on a repetitive basis, more frequently, with more severe impact. We had across the world this climate phenomenon becoming more of a threat to countries. In the case of Jamaica, we actually did provide emergency financing. We were part of a coordination group with the World Bank, the Inter-American Development Bank, CAF, and of course under the leadership of the Jamaican government. The Prime Minister personally took it upon himself to make sure that international support is well-coordinated and as impactful as possible. So what can Jamaica do, what can countries do? First, it is important to recognize we're not going to go back; we must go forward in a more climate-sensitive way. For economies like Jamaica, it is crucial to build resilience to these shocks, both in terms of how infrastructure is constructed, but also in terms of fiscal buffers and insurance products. Jamaica is one of the leaders of using insurance, and it helped tremendously. And I believe that the Caribbean, which has been doing quite a lot, should work even more closely together. And this is one piece of good news that I see in the Caribbean and across the world: regional cooperation and regional integration. Small countries cannot easily access insurance products alone, but when they pull together, they can do it. And learning from each other, taking those precautionary measures together, but also finding opportunities to strengthen growth, work in a way that enhances the viability of their economies. This is a very positive development, and we have been supporting it and we will continue to support them. And just to say, for all of us here in this room, it's tough when you are in a big country, but imagine what it is to be on an island. Imagine what it is if you are on an island in the Caribbean or in the Pacific and you are on the end of a very long chain—the Pacific is even in a tougher place—a very long supply chain. So as we think of our difficulties, spare a thought for those most at risk. All right. I'm going to go over here. Woman at the very end here. Yes. Thank you. Hello. I am Catherine Chan from Business World in the Philippines. So Managing Director, you've touched on the looming oil shortages in Asia, but I wanted to just zoom in a little bit: how is this conflict affecting ASEAN economies? And of course, given these shocks, what policy steps now should ASEAN governments prioritize to weather these shocks and boost their resilience as well as possibly attract investment amid this? Thank you. ASEAN countries have been very determined to put in place good policies and strong institutions. They have done it over the last years. Actually, ASEAN is a bright spot in terms of growth and in terms of economic dynamism. When we look at the impact of this shock, because of this strong build-up over the years, ASEAN is actually weathering the shock as a group of countries relatively well. But there is a big difference across ASEAN countries. Most ASEAN countries are energy importers. There are some that are exporters; they have that easier position. For the energy importers, those that had very little to none in terms of reserves of oil and gas, the situation is much more difficult. And I very much sympathize with the people in the Philippines because I know that your country does face that difficulty. ASEAN has chosen a path to regional integration and more regional trade, and more cooperation on the big transformative trends in the world—on technology, on AI, and on trade. They have taken very important steps to strengthen their interconnectivity. The Philippines is now leading the ASEAN. I am going to be there when the meeting takes place, and I do believe that this is very important for regions that have the potential to trade more within the countries of the region. Build that integration—you will benefit from it in a more shock-prone world. All right. I'm going to take one final question. I'm going to go right here, front row, the woman in the black blazer first row. Thank you very much. Karishma here from Business Today India Today group. Managing Director, you spoke about the ASEAN economy. I want to just take a broader regional look for Asia Pacific, especially the fact that the Economic Outlook report did raise the fact that the regional outlook is weakening, but with high hopes on countries like China and India. And I know you've been always optimistic about India saying that it's a resilient economy. If the situation gets severe, do you think emerging economies like India and also advanced economies like China will be able—will have the appetite to bear the brunt? The most impressive development over the last decades has been the advancement in policies and institutions in emerging market economies. We did a review of monetary and fiscal policy in emerging market economies. In monetary policy, they are as good—many of them, of course there are exceptions—but the majority are as good or better than advanced economies. In fiscal policy, there is still some space for further improvements, but countries are determined to move in that way. So we have a more resilient world because of the resilience of many emerging market economies that have put in place independent central banks, fiscal councils, and built very strong reserves. They benefit from it, but there is also a positive spillover for everybody else. In an adverse scenario, of course everybody would face a more difficult time. And if we are to go—if the impact of hostilities is to be prolonged, then we are going to see a slowdown in global growth. As I said in my opening, it could go all the way to 2%. And yet we would see economies that have these strong fundamentals and have dynamism, like India does; they would be performing better. Look at India today. India's growth is more than two times higher than the average global growth. And that comes because of this strength of fundamentals. So we do not see a scenario under which there would be a dramatic development. Of course, there is one issue that we all need to be very watchful of, and it is financial stability, that for now seems to be holding relatively well. Let it be that way in the future. And I can add to this, Asia is very severely impacted because of that dependency on imports. But we see Asia once again demonstrating that good performance historically is helping. And that story of relentless pursuit of good policies and building strong institutions is what we see in Asia. All right. I'm going to take one final question. I'm going to go right here, front row, the woman in the mint green. Thank you. I'm Tao Xu with China Central Television. My question is about China. So with global growth becoming more uncertain, can China help keep the world economy stable, and where do you see the biggest opportunity for international cooperation? It is a continuation of the answer I gave to the previous question. What we see in China, while we are slightly downgrading projections for growth from 4.5 to 4.4 percent, we do see resilience in the economy. China has great potential as it moves to change its growth model from one that is mostly dependent on exports to one that is primarily oriented toward domestic consumption, moving from goods to services. That can give a big boost to China. And of course, China's size means that when China does well, there are positive spillovers for the rest of the world, for the region, especially for Asia, but also for the rest of the world. All right. Thank you very much, everyone, and thank you, Managing Director. This concludes our press briefing. The full transcript of the press briefing will be made available on our website, and of course if you have further questions, please reach out to my colleagues at Media Relations. And we wish you all a wonderful day today. Thank you.

Exclusive: ECB President Lagarde Speaks to Bloomberg

Bloomberg Television(3.1M) ·22:46 ·4.9K views · 2026-04-15
Christine Lagarde (President, European Central Bank) Francine Lacqua (Anchor, Bloomberg Television)
> "We central bankers are left with defining the baseline...
TL;DR: ECB President Christine Lagarde characterizes the current European economy as sitting between "baseline" and "adverse" scenarios due to significant energy shocks, asserting that the ECB will remain agile and data-dependent rather than committed to a pre-defined rate path....

TL;DR: ECB President Christine Lagarde characterizes the current European economy as sitting between "baseline" and "adverse" scenarios due to significant energy shocks, asserting that the ECB will remain agile and data-dependent rather than committed to a pre-defined rate path.

Economic Outlook and Forecasting Scenarios

  • Current Projections: The ECB has revised its growth forecast down to 0.9% and its inflation forecast to 2.6%.
  • Scenario Modeling: Lagarde identifies three tiers for the economy: Baseline, Adverse, and Severe.
      • Current Status: The Eurozone is currently positioned between the baseline and the adverse scenario, largely dictated by fluctuating oil and gas prices.
  • Economic Fragility: Lagarde notes that while recovery was underway post-pandemic, the war-induced energy shock created massive fragmentation. She identifies Asia as the "first victim" in terms of economic losses, though Europe faces significant direct impacts.
  • The "Schizophrenic" Mandate: Central bankers must perform the dual task of focusing on medium-term price stability while monitoring highly volatile daily data, such as ship movements and spot oil prices.

Monetary Policy and Strategy

  • Data Dependency: Lagarde emphasizes that the ECB is not currently "predicating" a specific direction for interest rates. Any policy moves will be determined by whether incoming data suggests shocks are "short-lived/see-through" or "long-lasting."
  • Decision Framework: The Governing Council is watching for "secondary effects" that could de-anchor inflation expectations. If inflation peaks higher and persists, "decisive" action will be taken.
  • Coordination with Fiscal Policy: Lagarde urges political leaders to ensure fiscal support is "Targeted, Temporary, Transparent, and Tailored" (the T-T-T-T framework) to avoid working against monetary price stability.
  • Commitment to Target: Despite various scenarios reaching up to 4% inflation, Lagarde insists the ECB’s "compass" remains a firm 2% medium-term target.

Geopolitical Risks and Energy Infrastructure

  • The Strait of Hormuz: Identified as the ultimate "worst-case scenario" for global growth. A closure would block 20% of the world’s oil and gas, as well as critical ingredients for fertilizers and microchips.
  • Recovery Timelines:
      • Shipping/Distribution: Experts suggest a 2-3 month window to resume regular shipping conditions following a truce.
      • Refineries: Infrastructure damage (specifically citing facilities in Qatar and Saudi Arabia) could take years, not months, to repair.
  • Market Dichotomy: Lagarde warns of a "dichotomy" in markets between those acting as if it is "business as usual" and the reality of a significant, long-lasting shock to supply chains and food prices.

Structural Threats: AI and Political Shifts

  • Artificial Intelligence: Lagarde identifies AI as a "big issue" that concerns her more than immediate market fluctuations.
      • Social Impact: She questions the long-term effects on productivity, mass unemployment, and the "fiscal equilibrium" required for reskilling workers.
      • Governance Gap: She notes a lack of a global governance framework to manage the risks of AI falling into the "wrong hands."
  • Political Change in Hungary: Lagarde welcomed the victory of Péter Magyar, noting his two-thirds majority could remove obstacles to growth.
      • Euro Adoption: She noted Magyar’s interest in Hungary joining the Eurozone, describing it as the "natural path," though she cautioned that the convergence process takes years of judicial and legal reform.
  • Leadership Continuity: Responding to rumors about her tenure, Lagarde stated, "The captain does not leave the ship" during times of major disruption.

***

"We central bankers are left with defining the baseline... and because this information that we have is constantly changing and we have a high degree of unpredictability and uncertainty, we have to work on scenarios." — Christine Lagarde
"I take the see-through story into account, but this is not something that you can actually explain to the fisherman who cannot go at sea because he cannot pay for his petrol." — Christine Lagarde
"When there are big clouds on the horizon, the captain does not leave the ship. And this captain is not going to leave the ship." — Christine Lagarde
We were on a great path and were well positioned, so we had recovery underway. We were going to increase our projection, most likely. Inflation had been under control, oscillating around our 2% target for, you know, almost a year. Unemployment was at rock bottom, and employment participation was still significantly high. And then the war started. That created a situation of, I think, increased fragmentation. Everybody talks about the asymmetry of the shock, which is one of the largest energy shocks that we have ever seen on a global basis. And with that going on, I think that there was a movement of more integration. So you have a fragmented world, which becomes, I think, more fragmented—more fragmentation, and determination to better integrate. So we are at this interesting moment where there is damage caused, there is value lost, and there is destruction. I don't think that Europe is the epicenter of that. It's clearly Asia, which is the first victim of the situation in terms of economic losses. And I don't think that we should be forgetting the direct losses of people having died, being injured, and being displaced. There is the destruction that has taken place that will require reconstruction efforts. And that, I think, is and should be at the front of our mind as we look at the economic consequences. On the world economy, how much of a drag does it have on GDP forecasts? Again, oil prices fluctuate so much. You know, the direct impact on GDP is going to be measured by the IMF, the World Bank, and the OECD to a lesser extent because it doesn't have the whole constituency. But all of them are revisiting downward their projection. I think it's minus 0.3 compared with what they had expected. We are revisiting as well our growth forecast and our inflation forecast. We have published that, as you know, and we have revised inflation to 2.6% and growth to 0.9. And then the evolution is going to be a factor of how long, how deep, and how propagated the crisis is. We central bankers are left with defining the baseline, which is based on the most accurate information we have regarding what we think will happen. And because this information that we have is constantly changing and we have a high degree of unpredictability and uncertainty, we have to work on scenarios. We factor in how high oil and gas and all succeeding derivatives of those products will be, how long it will last, and how fast the situation will be recovered. We keep looking at current data as they come in. Thanks to Bloomberg, for instance, this comes in on a very regular basis. We try to figure out where we are relative to the baseline and where we are relative to the various scenarios that we have laid out. And I think you laid it out as three scenarios: baseline, adverse, and severe. Yeah, I would call the baseline the baseline, and the other two the scenarios. So where are we now? We are in between the baseline and the adverse. So we have baseline, adverse, and severe. Currently, when I say currently, you have to look at the price of the barrel, the price of the various categories of fuel, and the price of futures. All of that applies to gas as well to determine where we are exactly relative to these two. But we are somewhere, I think, in between the baseline and the adverse scenario. How quickly can that change from baseline to scenarios? It changes every day. The difficulty for us central bankers is that we need to take a medium-term view. This is how we define our target. This price stability that defines our mandate is done by reference to the medium-term results. And at the same time, facts, data, the number of ships blocked here, and the volume of oil extracted varies almost on a daily basis. So we need to do this somehow schizophrenic exercise of keeping our eyes on the medium term and making sure that we deliver on our mandate, but at the same time checking the data almost daily. So what does it mean for monetary policy? Does the baseline already warrant a hike? What it means for monetary policy is that we have to be two things. We have to be completely agile and ready to move in the direction that is required. And second, we have to be data-dependent, as we have repeatedly said. But it does not predicate, as we speak today, Francine, that we will go in one direction or the other. It certainly doesn't determine a rate path that I can confirm today. And any of the colleagues who are confident that it's going to be one way or the other, I don't know, honestly. Does the ECB have a tight... But don't forget one thing. The baseline and all our scenarios are designed and formulated with the incorporation of what the market assumes. And no other monetary policy or fiscal policy decision affecting the baseline or either of the two scenarios. Do you think there's a tightening bias at the ECB? No, I think we have a compass, which indicates price stability predicated on financial stability. Those are the two. I wouldn't call them bias. How different is it from the shock in 2022? And how different will your response be? Well, it's different from 2022 in many ways. If you look back, inflation was completely different, interest rates were completely different, and the shock was a combination of supply and demand. It was a vastly different situation. And I think Europe is affected in a much more significant way than it is at the moment. Because we had this sudden stop that came out of the war—the unjustifiable war of Russia against Ukraine—which brought to a halt the delivery of gas. That was very sudden. At the time, central banks were blamed for acting too slow. Is there a danger? I mean, how does that impact again your thinking this time? Look, I think we've said very clearly that we would need data to act, but that we would not hesitate to act. I think it really captures well the position that we have. We need the data in order to analyze whether it's a see-through situation that is going to be short-lived. We will get back to the past if you will, although I don't think that's actually possible. And we need all that. So it's either the see-through, which doesn't require that we take any particular decision, or it's going to be long-lasting. It will peak higher, and it will require action on our part. Or it will be somewhere in between, which will then require judgment on the part of the Governing Council to decide whether or not the indirect effect and the risk of secondary effect might de-anchor expectations, which then would require action as well. So I was at a dinner yesterday with the Treasury Secretary who was saying that actually he thinks this will be short-lived. But he's a political leader, as well as being a terribly competent person. I am not a political leader anymore. And our job as a central banker is to stay in our line and to observe our framework. We have one, thank you very much. And we must identify whether or not we are in a situation of short-lived, look-through, or long-lasting action, and possibly act decisively. Or, if we are in between, that requires a very subtle identification of how much and when, in order to make sure that inflation expectations are anchored and we do not get the secondary effects. So that's the job. It's the difficulty of what we have to do at the moment. We must very seriously look at the medium term while being attentive to the short term. And so your gut feeling for what we're living through now—you'll act quicker than in 2022? I don't have a gut feeling. You know, when I hear the music of "this is short-lived, we will go through that, you can see through," first of all, we decide what we have to do. Point number one. Point number two: you know, to explain to the fisherman who cannot go to sea because he cannot pay for his petrol, or when I think of the households that don't know whether they'll be able to pay for the refilling of their fuel tank, or when I think of the guys who have to drive to work and see the price of oil at the pump going up and up and up. I take the see-through story into account, but this is not something that you can actually explain. Which requires, from our perspective, in order to align in the most effective way monetary and fiscal policies, that we have a dialogue with the fiscal policy leaders. We ask them to be targeted and temporary, transparent, and tailored—and all those T-T-T-T—when they design support packages or help for the people who are the fisherman, the housekeeper, the household, or the guy who is driving his car. Given where we are now, what does a worst-case scenario for growth and inflation look like? The worst-case scenario is war, in any event. You know, as former head of the IMF, I have seen successful programs being completely wiped out by war. So war is, in any circumstances, the worst outcome. If you talk in strict economic terms and the movement of goods and all that, it's the Strait of Hormuz not being open for shipping and transportation. Because you have 20% of gas and oil going through that, as well as the likes of ingredients necessary for fertilizers and ingredients necessary for microchips. So what's a pain threshold for the Euro Area economy that the ECB would have to act on? Is it inflation at 3, 4% or is it... Look, we have forecasts for inflation that vary between 2.6, which is the baseline that we have, and a little north of 4%. Based on what I told you earlier, which is either a see-through or a strong, decisive reaction, or somewhere in between depending on the factors that we see, we will adjust because we will be agile. But one thing for sure is that we will keep our 2%. Madame Lagarde, how worried are you about the discrepancy between spot and future oil prices and so potentially tighter markets that they were pricing in? I think it's the whole dilemma that we have about short term and medium term. The more anxious and worried economic actors are about the resolution of this crisis, the more pressure there will be on the spot market. How much do you blame the US administration for what's happening to the European economy? You know, it's not my job to point fingers or to associate this or that with blame. I'm more in the hope section myself. And I very much hope that people can sit at the table, can come to their senses, and understand that it will be in the best interest of the global economy and of the people that a war settlement is reached. Not just a ceasefire, but that we can navigate through the straits of the world. It has a ramification with international law, but let's not go there. I'm a reformed lawyer, as you know. We won't go there, if you won't. I mean, is it something, when you look at the economic fallout actually in general, even if there's a truce or if there's lasting peace, how long does that play out for? I think there are two dimensions to that. One is for shipping to resume under regular, normal conditions; it will take, I don't know, whether it's two or three months. I listen to the experts and I try to understand how long it takes to fill in the ships again, to move the tankers around, and to deliver. I think most of them are saying that it's a two or three months job for the shipping and the distribution. Production refinery, that seems to be another matter. And when you look at the Ras Tanura refinery facility in Qatar, they say that it's not months, it's years that we're talking about. Some of the facilities in Saudi, where there's pipelines, probably shorter. But some of the other facilities will require long-term fixing and adjusting and repairing and setting it up again. I mean, given all of this, is there too much financial exuberance in markets? You know what is a little strange is that there is a tendency by some to assume that it's business as usual. And I think there is a bit of a dichotomy between those who regard business as usual and those on the other hand who are saying, "Watch out, it's a very significant shock." And we are not about to see the end of that process because there will be ramifications. There will be an impact on the price of processed and unprocessed food, and there will be an impact on the supply chain if it lasts longer. We're not going to see exactly the same disruption as during COVID, but something that will be in between normal and COVID disruption. But do you worry about financial stability? I mean, there's also private markets... I always do worry about financial stability and price stability because they are intrinsically interdependent. So, you know, we in the supervision function of the ECB and all supervisors around the world have to be very attentive to financial risks, whether declared and disclosed risks, which hopefully have mitigating factors associated with them. At least that's what the regulation is doing. And the rampant risks, the various, you know, black swans that are there. What worries you the most in all of this? You know, it's what worries me both in good and bad—and I hope we'll never have the ugly—it is the impact of artificial intelligence on our economies and the outcome and the governance of artificial intelligence. And what I mean by that is what impact will it have, not just on productivity, where our stats will go, which obviously matters to us central bankers, but what impact will it have on our societies? How many people will be unemployed? How many people will require retraining and reskilling? Who will pay for that? The whole fiscal equilibrium that will result from the massive transformation that is expected in many corners. That keeps me—not awake at night because I try to get some sleep—but it's a big issue. It's a really big issue. Are governments thinking about that given they're also dealing with this massive energy crisis? You know, I've been in government a few years of my life. What is really difficult is to focus on the day-to-day, to mind the next election, and to respect your program or the expectations that your voters have. And yet at the same time, you must anticipate what will be the effect of major breakthroughs, innovations, and significant developments. And I think that in that category, AI is something that we need to have at the front of our mind because it is moving so fast. The impact of it can be so disruptive for good or for bad. I think the development we've seen with Anthropic and Mistral is a good example of a responsible company that is suddenly thinking, "that could be really good, but if it falls in the wrong hands, it could be really bad." We need to do something about it. I don't think there is a framework. You know, everybody is keen to have a framework within which to operate. I don't think there is a governance framework that is there to actually mind those things. We need to work on that. Do you think financial institutions are prepared for... Well, I think they've been called to meetings in various corners of the world, and I know that we at the ECB will be talking, are talking to financial institutions to alert them to those risks. Yes. Madame Lagarde, we also had elections in Hungary over the weekend. What does Orban's defeat mean for Europe and the world? I think I'm looking at Péter Magyar's victory, which was a significant landslide result. He has a two-thirds majority. He can change... this new Prime Minister can change the constitution, remove a number of obstacles to both growth and integrity in the governance of that country. And I think that that is really to be welcomed. The second thing that it tells me is that Hungary is in the European family. And what I heard recently is that this new Prime Minister, Péter Magyar, is indicating that he would like to see Hungary join the euro area and the forint of Hungary be replaced by the euro. Which I think is the natural path of all 27... well, 26, actually, it excludes Denmark. But 26 member states are expected to be part of the family. And we have 21. If Hungary goes through the process of integration, convergence, and all of that, it would be a great achievement. It would coincide with what was expected by the founders. Do you think that the talks on adoption of the euro by Hungary will happen in your mandate? Wow. You'd have to extend my mandate a little bit, which I think is not in the cards. I'm happy to stay, but... no. Because convergence requires a number of reforms, some of which I think Hungary is going to consider and hopefully implement. The judicial one is a point in case. And then it requires an adjustment period, which is usually anywhere between two and a few more years depending on which country you look at. But the convergence process takes a bit of time. You know, you need to align a lot of parameters; you need to have the same legal basis on many aspects. But I'm delighted that he's looking at it with a very positive approach and he's speaking up on that basis. Talking about your mandate, will you see it out? Will you fully finish your mandate? Look, when there are big clouds on the horizon, the captain does not leave the ship. And this captain is not going to leave the ship, because I see clouds. Biggest cloud? Well, we know them. We're all facing the same situation, with asymmetry as I indicated, but when you see major disruption, the energy supply being reduced, when you see threats to growth, upside risk to inflation—these are serious matters. We have to be attentive to and to keep under control so that our price stability, our 2% target, is not going to be varied from. Madame Lagarde, Captain Lagarde, thank you so much for joining us. Thank you. Thank you so much. Thank you again, Madame Lagarde and Francine Lacqua for that terrific interview. If you could all remain in your seats while Madame Lagarde exits. And again, we appreciate your time. Thank you so much.

Iran War: Trump Says End to War in Sight, Spurring Market Rally | The Opening Trade 4/15/2026

Bloomberg Television(3.1M) ·96:09 ·8.4K views · 2026-04-15
Donald Trump (US President)
Global markets are rallying on signals from President Trump that the war with Iran may be nearing an end, with investors increasingly prioritizing the "AI boom" and resilient US consumer data over the ongoing shipping crisis in the Strait of Hormuz.
TL;DR Global markets are rallying on signals from President Trump that the war with Iran may be nearing an end, with investors increasingly prioritizing the "AI boom" and resilient US consumer data over the ongoing shipping crisis in the Strait of Hormuz. War De-escalation:...

TL;DR

Global markets are rallying on signals from President Trump that the war with Iran may be nearing an end, with investors increasingly prioritizing the "AI boom" and resilient US consumer data over the ongoing shipping crisis in the Strait of Hormuz.

Geopolitical Outlook & Market Reaction

  • War De-escalation: Market optimism is growing as President Trump indicates a potential wind-down of the conflict with Iran, shifting the narrative toward a post-war economic recovery.
  • Asset Performance: Major indices, including the S&P 500 and various Asian exchanges (CSI 300, Singapore), have returned to pre-war levels.
  • Treasury Positioning: Markets are heavily positioning for a drop in 10-year yields to the 4%–4.2% range, suggesting that fears of war-driven inflation are being replaced by growth optimism.
  • Currency Shifts: The US dollar has surrendered most of its "war gains" as the narrative shifts. However, Deutsche Bank notes that the US consumer may face higher relative gas prices compared to regions with more controlled pump prices.

Energy Crisis & The Strait of Hormuz

  • Ongoing Blockade: Despite market optimism, the Strait of Hormuz remains officially closed due to blockades. Current traffic is described as a "fraction" of the normal 130 vessels per day.
  • Supply Shortages: Experts warn that global shortages are building the longer the blockade persists, even if "worst-case" scenarios have not fully materialized yet due to lags in the supply chain.
  • Testing the Blockade:
      • Iran is reportedly considering a short-term pause in exports to avoid direct military tests of the blockade.
      • The *Agios Fanourios I* is currently testing the Iranian reaction by heading to Basra to load Iraqi crude for Vietnam; the Trump administration has permitted the move as it is not an Iranian-focused vessel.

Corporate Earnings: Tech vs. Luxury

  • Semiconductors & AI: The tech sector remains the primary engine of market growth, with Guy Johnson noting the current market is essentially a battle of "Strait of Hormuz vs. AI."
      • ASML: Shares surged on positive full-year sales guidance and increased guidance. The company reports being "sold out" and supply-constrained, though Q2 outlook remains cautious.
  • Luxury Sector Disruption: Luxury brands are seeing an indirect hit from the conflict due to tourism disruption.
      • Hermes: Q1 sales grew 5.6% but missed estimates. Markets like the UK, Italy, and Thailand were negatively impacted by a decline in Middle Eastern tourism.
      • Kering: Continuing to struggle with the turnaround of the Gucci brand, though non-Gucci businesses showed some resilience.

Banking & Macro Indicators

  • US Bank Resilience: Recent earnings from JP Morgan, Wells Fargo, Citi, and BlackRock show a divided stock reaction but a consistent theme of high profitability from market turbulence.
  • "Nice Volatility": Bank leaders suggest that current market swings have provided profitable trading conditions without the "gappy" instability seen in previous crises.
  • Upcoming Catalysts:
      • IMF Spring Meetings: Ongoing discussions in Washington D.C.
      • Central Bank Speakers: Key commentary expected from the BoE’s Bailey, ECB’s Schnabel, and various Fed officials (Barr, Bowman).
      • Fed Beige Book: Scheduled for release at 7:00 PM UK time to provide more clarity on US economic health.

Notable Quotes

"The narrative coming out of the company [ASML] is that actually, we're sold out. We are supply-constrained, and we can't deliver whatever everybody wants." — Guy Johnson, Bloomberg Host
"It’s still closed. The shortages are building. The longer this thing goes on for, the longer we’re going to have a problem for... it's tech versus the Strait of Hormuz at the moment." — Guy Johnson, Bloomberg Host
"The dollar has given up most of its war gains... maybe [the US] is paying more of a consumer price than some other places in the world where the price at the pump is more controlled." — Anna Edwards, Bloomberg Host
"This isn't just a Middle East story that stayed in the Middle East in terms of its impact on travel and therefore luxury." — Anna Edwards, Bloomberg Host
