He Studied $6.4 Trillion Worth of Founders. Here's What They Shared | Mark Macleod | Traction

Traction(12.9K) ·55:28 ·798 views · 2026-04-15
Mark Macleod (Ex-CFO, Shopify and FreshBooks)
The primary currency of leadership is energy, not hours worked; startups ultimately fail when founders run out of energy, making personal physical, mental, and spiritual health a strategic business imperative rather than a luxury.
TL;DR The primary currency of leadership is energy, not hours worked; startups ultimately fail when founders run out of energy, making personal physical, mental, and spiritual health a strategic business imperative rather than a luxury. Mark MacLeod’s study of 11 founders...

TL;DR

The primary currency of leadership is energy, not hours worked; startups ultimately fail when founders run out of energy, making personal physical, mental, and spiritual health a strategic business imperative rather than a luxury.

The Trillion-Dollar Correlation: Health as a Scaling Strategy

Mark MacLeod’s study of 11 founders (including Tobi Lütke, Jeff Bezos, and Mark Zuckerberg) who built a combined $6.4 trillion in value revealed a consistent pattern: elite performance is tied to rigorous personal maintenance.

  • Physicality as Strategy: High-performing CEOs often prioritize physical health as they scale. Examples include Jeff Bezos’s physical transformation and Brian Chesky’s background in competitive bodybuilding.
  • The "Deadlift ETF": MacLeod notes an informal study showing that stocks of CEOs who lift weights (e.g., Zuckerberg, Bezos) outperformed the S&P 500 by 2.5x over four years.
  • The Zone of Genius: Successful founders like Tobi Lütke (Shopify) protect their energy by delegating tasks that drain them (e.g., Wall Street relations) to partners like Harley Finkelstein, allowing the CEO to stay in their "zone of genius" (product and technology).

The "Limitless" Leadership Philosophy

The "Limitless" framework rejects "hustle porn" and the narrative of "sleep when I’m dead," arguing that these are the primary drivers of startup failure.

  • Nervous System as Operating System: A founder’s internal state dictates company culture. If a founder is "fried," they will inevitably build a "fried" and reactive culture.
  • The Three Pillars of Energy:
      • Physical: Prioritizing sleep (the #1 performance hack), real food, and weightlifting (especially for those over 40).
      • Mental: Cultivating "presence" to avoid "time traveling"—the act of simultaneously reliving past mistakes and fearing future ones.
      • Spiritual: Using practices like Kundalini yoga or meditation to tap into intuition and "flow," which MacLeod describes as "spirit is infinite potential."
  • Acknowledging Limits: Paradoxically, the path to being limitless begins with acknowledging physical and mental boundaries to avoid burnout.

Operational Excellence: CEO Altitude and Friction

As a company scales, the CEO's role must shift from "doing" to "clearing the path."

  • The Three Core CEO Tasks: Citing Fred Wilson (Union Square Ventures), MacLeod defines the CEO role at scale as:

1. Communicating the vision/mission.

2. Building the strongest possible senior leadership team.

3. Ensuring the company never runs out of money.

  • Removing Friction: The CEO should act like "good cholesterol" (HDL), moving through the company to identify and remove friction in products, processes, and teams.
  • The Dashboard Approach: Founders should track biomarkers (HRV, sleep scores, blood glucose, and visceral fat) with the same rigor they apply to SaaS KPIs. Changes in these metrics often predict business failures months in advance.

Integrating Personal and Professional Longevity

Sustainable leadership requires intentionality in personal relationships and the "witness state."

  • Work-Life Integration: Excellence is never "balanced" (e.g., LeBron James is not "balanced"). Instead of seeking balance, founders should seek "presence"—being 100% home when they are home.
  • Marriage Meetings: MacLeod recommends a structured, weekly "marriage meeting" agenda:
      • Gratitude: Acknowledging what you noticed in your partner.
      • Tasks/Chores: Managing the "operations" of the household.
      • Issue Resolution: A safe forum for addressing conflicts before they metastasize.
  • Values Alignment: Conflict often arises not from a lack of love, but from misaligned "operating systems" (e.g., one partner valuing high-agency/risk while the other values stability).

Notable Quotes

"Startups die when founders run out of energy. The number one reason why people come to me looking to exit is because they are burnt out." — Mark MacLeod
"Your nervous system is your company operating system. If you're fried, you're gonna build a fried culture." — Mark MacLeod
"The healthy person has a hundred priorities. The sick person has one priority, which is to be healthy. When you're sick, you realize that nothing else matters." — Mark MacLeod
"Pain equals suffering times resistance. There will be suffering in life... whether you experience pain is only a function of whether you resist that suffering." — Mark MacLeod

So after watching brilliant founders burn themselves out at the exact time their companies needed them the most, Mark has rebuilt his entire philosophy around one idea: that energy is the real currency of leadership. And today he's just launched, or you've been running, Limitless, where CEOs train like elite athletes so they can scale companies without sacrificing their health, their families, and build a sustainable long-term legacy. He's one of the rare operators who understands that your company will grow to your level of personal capacity. So I have a personal story with Mark. A few years ago, I had a massive health scare just weeks after a majority sale of my company. What was meant to be sort of a celebration turned into a nightmare for my family and me. We had just had a majority sale of the company and the wire hits my bank account, so I booked everyone for a flight to Bora Bora. The morning of Bora Bora I end up in the hospital; my lungs collapse. And after I came out of the hospital, like, depression hit me. I saw, like, my roles getting replaced. I couldn't find my footing in my own company. And I had one conversation with Mark after I got out of the hospital, and it changed how I thought about human performance and it really kicked off a journey of personal transformation. And you'll see I was, like, overweight, 250 pounds almost, and I got into this whole mental, spiritual, physical transformation. So if you care about building something for decades and not just surviving the next round of funding, this episode is for you. So let's get into it. Mark MacLeod: the man, the myth, the legend. Thanks for joining us. Thanks for having me, Lloyed. You are in Tulum, very interesting. You spend, you said you spend three months of the year in Tulum. Now you've been a CFO, investor, founder, and now a CEO coach. What led you to Limitless? Yeah, it's been a long journey, that's for sure. I gave a talk at Turing Fest in Scotland either last summer or two summers ago, I forget. Anyway, the talk was called Limitless Leadership, where I studied 11 founders who had built companies worth 6.4 trillion dollars. The shiny names you know: Elon, Tobi, Brian Chesky, etc. And setting aside luck and timing, I wanted to identify what were their leadership styles and management practices. I wanted to reverse engineer their success and then present those insights. And as I was flying back, I realized, like, actually my purpose has always been to help CEOs become limitless, right? Whether I was a CFO acting as a right hand to a CEO, a VC trying to help founders become CEOs, a banker trying to help them achieve life-changing outcomes, or now obviously as a coach, that's my purpose, right? Like, I see the purpose of coaching as helping you to always be growing faster than your business. And you know, we have this image in our mind of a stereotypical founder, right? Like, they got the hoodie, they just go into the night, "sleep when I die," hustle culture—that's like the dominant narrative. And we actually celebrate busyness in society, right? When we catch up with someone after a while and we ask how they are, often they were like, "Yeah, I'm so busy," and they're like, "Yeah, yeah, I'm so busy." It's like this hustle porn. And you know, Naval Ravikant, co-founder of AngelList, says it best, right? Startups die when founders run out of energy. And I've personally sold over a billion dollars worth of companies and the number one reason why people came to me looking to exit is because they were burnt out. They just couldn't go on. Now they could have theoretically replaced themselves and the company could continue, but there are a few issues with that. One, like, all of their net worth is tied up in these illiquid shares. Two, if they were doing it long enough, they're unemployable, like they just can't work for someone else. And then three, for better or worse, their identity is tied up in this company and so their conclusion is like, "I just need to wrap this up," right? And so long story short, Limitless is basically a reaction to this default hustle culture, "sleep when I die" narrative, which is actually the thing that causes the bulk of startup failure, right? So yeah, we can get into it but it's a reaction to that and is basically the opposite of that. And I have learned through my own journey and also through working with CEOs for a very long time that there's a better way, a more sustainable way. And I noticed, going back to this talk that I gave called Limitless Leadership, that with the exception of Elon Musk, who's the exception to most rules, almost every other founder I studied, at some point along the way—maybe not at the very beginning, but at some point when they really settled into scaling—started prioritizing physical health. You probably noticed, like, Zuckerberg's looking pretty jacked compared to at the beginning. Bezos could be a bouncer if he wasn't a billionaire. A lot of them have mindfulness practices. Brian Chesky from Airbnb has been practicing transcendental meditation for a long time. Melanie Perkins from Canva does morning walks; she journals every day. Like, they have these rituals that they wouldn't call self-care but they are self-care designed to give them mental clarity, and then they create boundaries. Like, Brian Chesky's a night owl so he doesn't really work in the mornings. He rolls into the office sometime before lunch but he stays late in the evening because that's what fits, kind of, his natural rhythms. And you know, they designed their role and therefore their leadership team and therefore their company around their superpower, right? And like the best example of that is Tobi from Shopify, right? Tobi runs a publicly traded company; he should theoretically be the person doing the Wall Street song and dance every quarter but he's not because it drains him. Meanwhile, his right hand Harley is built for that, right? Like Harley is literally purpose-built to be in front of a camera. And so, Tobi did that from the earliest days.
